From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia

English polymath (1642–1727)

For other uses, see [Isaac Newton (disambiguation)](/wiki/Isaac_Newton_\(disambiguation\) "Isaac Newton \(disambiguation\)").

Signature
[![Signature written in ink in a flowing script](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Isaac_Newton_signature_ws.svg/250px-Isaac_Newton_signature_ws.svg.png)](/wiki/File:Isaac_Newton_signature_ws.svg)

**Sir Isaac Newton** ([/ˈnjuːtən/](/wiki/Help:IPA/English "Help:IPA/English") [](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/64/LL-Q1860_%28eng%29-Naomi_Persephone_Amethyst_%28NaomiAmethyst%29-Newton.wav/LL-Q1860_%28eng%29-Naomi_Persephone_Amethyst_%28NaomiAmethyst%29-Newton.wav.mp3 "Play audio")[ⓘ](/wiki/File:LL-Q1860_\(eng\)-Naomi_Persephone_Amethyst_\(NaomiAmethyst\)-Newton.wav "File:LL-Q1860 \(eng\)-Naomi Persephone Amethyst \(NaomiAmethyst\)-Newton.wav"); 4 January [[O.S.](/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates "Old Style and New Style dates") 25 December] 1643 - 31 March [[O.S.](/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates "Old Style and New Style dates") 20 March] 1727)[a] was an English [polymath](/wiki/Polymath "Polymath") who was a mathematician, physicist, astronomer, alchemist, theologian, author and inventor.[5] He was a key figure in the [Scientific Revolution](/wiki/Scientific_Revolution "Scientific Revolution") and the [Enlightenment](/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment "Age of Enlightenment") that followed.[6] His book _[Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica](/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica "Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica")_ (_Mathematical Principles of Natural Philosophy_), first published in 1687, [achieved the first great unification in physics](/wiki/Unification_of_theories_in_physics#Unification_of_gravity_on_Earth_with_astronomical_behaviors "Unification of theories in physics") and established [classical mechanics](/wiki/Classical_mechanics "Classical mechanics").[7][8] Newton also made seminal contributions to [optics](/wiki/Optics "Optics"), and [shares credit](/wiki/Leibniz%E2%80%93Newton_calculus_controversy "Leibniz–Newton calculus controversy") with the German mathematician [Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz](/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz "Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz") for formulating [infinitesimal calculus](/wiki/Calculus "Calculus"), although he developed calculus years before Leibniz. Newton contributed to and refined the [scientific method](/wiki/Scientific_method "Scientific method"), and his work is considered the most influential in bringing forth modern science.

In the _Principia_ , Newton formulated the [laws of motion](/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion "Newton's laws of motion") and [universal gravitation](/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation "Newton's law of universal gravitation") that formed the dominant scientific viewpoint for centuries until it was superseded by the [theory of relativity](/wiki/Theory_of_relativity "Theory of relativity"). While this is the case, his laws still serve as excellent approximations for the vast majority of physical phenomena involving low speeds (much less than the [speed of light](/wiki/Speed_of_light "Speed of light")) and weak [gravitational fields](/wiki/Gravitational_field "Gravitational field"). He used his mathematical description of [gravity](/wiki/Gravity "Gravity") to derive [Kepler's laws of planetary motion](/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion "Kepler's laws of planetary motion"), account for [tides](/wiki/Tide "Tide"), the [trajectories](/wiki/Trajectory "Trajectory") of [comets](/wiki/Comet "Comet"), the [precession of the equinoxes](/wiki/Axial_precession "Axial precession") and other phenomena, eradicating doubt about the [Solar System](/wiki/Solar_System "Solar System")'s [heliocentricity](/wiki/Heliocentrism "Heliocentrism").[9] Newton solved the [two-body problem](/wiki/Two-body_problem "Two-body problem") and introduced the [three-body problem](/wiki/Three-body_problem "Three-body problem"). He demonstrated that the [motion of objects](/wiki/Dynamics_\(mechanics\) "Dynamics \(mechanics\)") on Earth and [celestial bodies](/wiki/Astronomical_object "Astronomical object") could be accounted for by the same principles. Newton's inference that the Earth is an [oblate spheroid](/wiki/Spheroid#Oblate_spheroids "Spheroid") was later confirmed by the geodetic measurements of [Alexis Clairaut](/wiki/Alexis_Clairaut "Alexis Clairaut"), [Charles Marie de La Condamine](/wiki/Charles_Marie_de_La_Condamine "Charles Marie de La Condamine"), and others, convincing most European scientists of the superiority of Newtonian mechanics over earlier systems. He was also the first to calculate the [age of Earth](/wiki/Age_of_Earth "Age of Earth") by experiment, and described a precursor to the modern [wind tunnel](/wiki/Wind_tunnel "Wind tunnel"). Further, he was the first to provide a quantitative estimate of the [solar mass](/wiki/Solar_mass "Solar mass").

Newton built the [first reflecting telescope](/wiki/Newtonian_telescope "Newtonian telescope") and developed a sophisticated [theory of colour](/wiki/Color_theory "Color theory") based on the observation that a [prism](/wiki/Dispersive_prism "Dispersive prism") separates [white light](/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum#Visible_radiation_\(light\) "Electromagnetic spectrum") into the colours of the [visible spectrum](/wiki/Visible_spectrum "Visible spectrum"). His work on light was collected in his book _[Opticks](/wiki/Opticks "Opticks")_ , published in 1704. He originated prisms as [beam expanders](/wiki/Beam_expander "Beam expander") and [multiple-prism arrays](/wiki/Multiple-prism_dispersion_theory "Multiple-prism dispersion theory"), which would later become integral to the development of [tunable lasers](/wiki/Tunable_laser "Tunable laser").[10] Newton invented a [double-reflecting quadrant](/wiki/Octant_\(instrument\) "Octant \(instrument\)") and was the first to theorise the [Goos–Hänchen effect](/wiki/Goos%E2%80%93H%C3%A4nchen_effect "Goos–Hänchen effect"). He also formulated an [empirical law of cooling](/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_cooling "Newton's law of cooling"), which was the first heat transfer formulation and serves as the formal basis of [convective heat transfer](/wiki/Convection_\(heat_transfer\) "Convection \(heat transfer\)"),[11] made the first theoretical calculation of the [speed of sound](/wiki/Speed_of_sound "Speed of sound"), and introduced the notions of a [Newtonian fluid](/wiki/Newtonian_fluid "Newtonian fluid") and a [black body](/wiki/Black_body "Black body"). He was also the first to explain the [Magnus effect](/wiki/Magnus_effect "Magnus effect"). Moreover, he was the first to analyse [Couette flow](/wiki/Couette_flow "Couette flow"). In addition to his creation of calculus, Newton's work on mathematics was extensive. He generalised the [binomial theorem](/wiki/Binomial_theorem "Binomial theorem") to any real number, introduced the [Puiseux series](/wiki/Puiseux_series "Puiseux series"), was the first to state [Bézout's theorem](/wiki/B%C3%A9zout%27s_theorem "Bézout's theorem"), classified most of the [cubic plane curves](/wiki/Cubic_plane_curve "Cubic plane curve"), contributed to the study of [Cremona transformations](/wiki/Cremona_transformation "Cremona transformation"), developed [a method](/wiki/Newton%27s_method "Newton's method") for approximating the [roots of a function](/wiki/Zero_of_a_function "Zero of a function"), originated the [Newton–Cotes formulas](/wiki/Newton%E2%80%93Cotes_formulas "Newton–Cotes formulas") used for [numerical integration](/wiki/Numerical_integration "Numerical integration"), and further produced the earliest explicit enunciation of the general [Taylor series](/wiki/Taylor_series "Taylor series"). Additionally, Newton initiated the field of [calculus of variations](/wiki/Calculus_of_variations "Calculus of variations"), formulated and solved the earliest problem in [geometric probability](/wiki/Geometric_probability "Geometric probability"), devised the earliest form of [linear regression](/wiki/Linear_regression "Linear regression"), and was a pioneer of [vector analysis](/wiki/Vector_calculus "Vector calculus").

Newton was a fellow of [Trinity College](/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge "Trinity College, Cambridge") and the second [Lucasian Professor of Mathematics](/wiki/Lucasian_Professor_of_Mathematics "Lucasian Professor of Mathematics") at the [University of Cambridge](/wiki/University_of_Cambridge "University of Cambridge"); he was appointed at the age of 26. He was a devout but unorthodox Christian who privately rejected the doctrine of the [Trinity](/wiki/Trinity "Trinity"). He refused to take [holy orders](/wiki/Holy_orders "Holy orders") in the [Church of England](/wiki/Church_of_England "Church of England"), unlike most members of the Cambridge faculty of the day. Beyond his work on the [mathematical sciences](/wiki/Mathematical_sciences "Mathematical sciences"), Newton dedicated much of his time to the study of [alchemy](/wiki/Alchemy "Alchemy") and [biblical chronology](/wiki/Chronology_of_the_Bible "Chronology of the Bible"), but most of his work in those areas remained unpublished until long after his death. Politically and personally tied to the [Whigs](/wiki/Whigs_\(British_political_party\) "Whigs \(British political party\)"), Newton served two brief terms as [Member of Parliament for the University of Cambridge](/wiki/Cambridge_University_\(UK_Parliament_constituency\) "Cambridge University \(UK Parliament constituency\)"), in 1689–1690 and 1701–1702. He was [knighted](/wiki/Knight "Knight") by [Queen Anne](/wiki/Anne,_Queen_of_Great_Britain "Anne, Queen of Great Britain") in 1705 and spent the last three decades of his life in London, serving as [Warden](/wiki/Warden_of_the_Mint "Warden of the Mint") (1696–1699) and [Master](/wiki/Master_of_the_Mint "Master of the Mint") (1699–1727) of the [Royal Mint](/wiki/Royal_Mint "Royal Mint"), in which he increased the accuracy and security of British coinage. He was also the president of the [Royal Society](/wiki/Royal_Society "Royal Society") (1703–1727).

## Early life

Main article: [Early life of Isaac Newton](/wiki/Early_life_of_Isaac_Newton "Early life of Isaac Newton")

Isaac Newton was born (according to the [Julian calendar](/wiki/Julian_calendar "Julian calendar") in use in England at the time) on Christmas Day, 25 December 1642 ([NS](/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates "Old Style and New Style dates") 4 January 1643[a]) at [Woolsthorpe Manor](/wiki/Woolsthorpe_Manor "Woolsthorpe Manor") in [Woolsthorpe-by-Colsterworth](/wiki/Woolsthorpe-by-Colsterworth "Woolsthorpe-by-Colsterworth"), a hamlet in [Lincolnshire](/wiki/Lincolnshire "Lincolnshire").[12] His father, also named Isaac Newton, had died three months before. Born prematurely, Newton was a small child; his mother, [Hannah Ayscough](/wiki/Hannah_Ayscough "Hannah Ayscough"), said that he could have fit inside a [quart](/wiki/Quart "Quart") mug.[13] When Newton was three, his mother remarried and went to live with her new husband, the Reverend Barnabas Smith, leaving her son in the care of his maternal grandmother, Margery Ayscough (née Blythe). Newton disliked his stepfather and maintained some enmity towards his mother for marrying him, as revealed by this entry in a list of sins committed up to the age of 19: "Threatening my father and mother Smith to burn them and the house over them."[14] Newton's mother had three children (Mary, Benjamin, and Hannah) from her second marriage.[15]

### The King's School

From the age of about twelve until he was seventeen, Newton was educated at [The King's School](/wiki/The_King%27s_School,_Grantham "The King's School, Grantham") in [Grantham](/wiki/Grantham "Grantham"), which taught [Latin](/wiki/Latin "Latin") and [Ancient Greek](/wiki/Ancient_Greek "Ancient Greek") and probably imparted a significant foundation of mathematics.[16] He was removed from school by his mother and returned to Woolsthorpe by October 1659. His mother, widowed for the second time, attempted to make him a farmer, an occupation he hated.[17] Henry Stokes, master at The King's School, and Reverend William Ayscough (Newton's uncle) persuaded his mother to send him back to school.[18] Motivated by a desire for revenge against a schoolyard bully, whom Newton beat in a fight and humiliated, he became the top-ranked student,[19] distinguishing himself mainly by building [sundials](/wiki/Sundial "Sundial") and models of windmills.[20]

### University of Cambridge

In June 1661, Newton was admitted to [Trinity College](/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge "Trinity College, Cambridge") at the [University of Cambridge](/wiki/University_of_Cambridge "University of Cambridge"). His uncle the Reverend William Ayscough, who had studied at Cambridge, recommended him to the university. At Cambridge, Newton started as a [subsizar](/wiki/Subsizar "Subsizar"), paying his way by performing [valet](/wiki/Valet "Valet") duties until he was awarded a scholarship in 1664, which covered his university costs for four more years until the completion of his [MA](/wiki/Master_of_Arts_\(Oxbridge_and_Dublin\) "Master of Arts \(Oxbridge and Dublin\)").[21] At the time, Cambridge's teachings were based on those of [Aristotle](/wiki/Aristotle "Aristotle"), whom Newton read along with then more modern philosophers, including [René Descartes](/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes "René Descartes") and [astronomers](/wiki/Astronomer "Astronomer") such as [Galileo Galilei](/wiki/Galileo_Galilei "Galileo Galilei") and [Thomas Street](/wiki/Thomas_Street_\(astronomer\) "Thomas Street \(astronomer\)"). He set down in his notebook a series of "[_Quaestiones_](/wiki/Quaestiones_quaedam_philosophicae "Quaestiones quaedam philosophicae")" about [mechanical philosophy](/wiki/Mechanical_philosophy "Mechanical philosophy") as he found it.[22] In 1665, he discovered the generalised [binomial theorem](/wiki/Binomial_theorem "Binomial theorem") and began to develop a mathematical theory that later became [calculus](/wiki/Calculus "Calculus"). Soon after Newton obtained his BA degree at Cambridge in August 1665, the university temporarily closed as a precaution against the [Great Plague](/wiki/Great_Plague_of_London "Great Plague of London").[23]

Although he had been undistinguished as a Cambridge student, his private studies and the years following his bachelor's degree have been described as "the richest and most productive ever experienced by a scientist".[24] The next two years alone saw the development of theories on calculus,[25] [optics](/wiki/Optics "Optics"), and the [law of gravitation](/wiki/Law_of_gravitation "Law of gravitation"), at his home in Woolsthorpe. The physicist Louis Trenchard More writes that "There are no other examples of achievement in the history of science to compare with that of Newton during those two golden years."[26]

Newton has been described as an "exceptionally organized" person when it came to note-taking, further [dog-earing](/wiki/Dog_ears "Dog ears") pages he saw as important. Furthermore, Newton's "indexes look like present-day indexes: They are alphabetical, by topic." His books showed his interests to be wide-ranging, with Newton himself described as a "Janusian thinker, someone who could mix and combine seemingly disparate fields to stimulate creative breakthroughs."[27] [William Stukeley](/wiki/William_Stukeley "William Stukeley") wrote that Newton "was not only very expert with his mechanical tools, but he was equally so with his pen", and further illustrated how Newton's lodging room wall at Grantham was covered in drawings of "birds, beasts, men, ships & mathematical schemes. & very well designed". He also noted his "uncommon skill & industry in mechanical works".[28]

In April 1667, Newton returned to the University of Cambridge, and in October he was elected as a fellow of Trinity.[29][30] Fellows were required to take [holy orders](/wiki/Holy_orders "Holy orders") and be ordained as [Anglican](/wiki/Anglicanism "Anglicanism") priests, although this was not enforced in the [Restoration](/wiki/Stuart_Restoration "Stuart Restoration") years, and an assertion of conformity to the [Church of England](/wiki/Church_of_England "Church of England") was sufficient. He made the commitment that "I will either set Theology as the object of my studies and will take holy orders when the time prescribed by these statutes [7 years] arrives, or I will resign from the college."[31] Up until this point he had not thought much about religion and had twice signed his agreement to the [Thirty-nine Articles](/wiki/Thirty-nine_Articles "Thirty-nine Articles"), the basis of Church of England doctrine. By 1675 the issue could not be avoided, and his unconventional views stood in the way.[32]

His academic work impressed the [Lucasian Professor](/wiki/Lucasian_Professor_of_Mathematics "Lucasian Professor of Mathematics") [Isaac Barrow](/wiki/Isaac_Barrow "Isaac Barrow"), who was anxious to develop his own religious and administrative potential (he became master of Trinity College two years later); in 1669, Newton succeeded him, only one year after receiving his MA. Newton argued that this should exempt him from the ordination requirement, and King [Charles II](/wiki/Charles_II_of_England "Charles II of England"), whose permission was needed, accepted this argument; thus, a conflict between Newton's religious views and Anglican orthodoxy was averted.[33] He was appointed at the age of 26.[34]

As accomplished as Newton was as a theoretician, he was less effective as a teacher; his classes were almost always empty. Humphrey Newton, his [sizar](/wiki/Sizar "Sizar") (assistant), noted that Newton would arrive on time and, if the room was empty, he would reduce his lecture time in half from 30 to 15 minutes, talk to the walls, then retreat to his experiments, thus fulfilling his contractual obligations. For his part Newton enjoyed neither teaching nor students. Over his career he was only assigned three students to tutor and none were noteworthy.[35]

Newton was elected a [Fellow of the Royal Society (FRS) in 1672](/wiki/List_of_fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_elected_in_1672 "List of fellows of the Royal Society elected in 1672").[1]

### Revision of _Geographia Generalis_

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Geographia_Generalis_1733_Figures_43%2C_44%2C_45%2C_46%2C_47%2C_48%2C_and_49.jpg/250px-Geographia_Generalis_1733_Figures_43%2C_44%2C_45%2C_46%2C_47%2C_48%2C_and_49.jpg)](/wiki/File:Geographia_Generalis_1733_Figures_43,_44,_45,_46,_47,_48,_and_49.jpg)Some of the figures added by Isaac Newton in his 1672 and 1681 editions of the _[Geographia Generalis](/wiki/Geographia_Generalis "Geographia Generalis")_. These figures appeared in subsequent editions as well.[36]

The Lucasian Professor of Mathematics at Cambridge position included the responsibility of instructing [geography](/wiki/Geography "Geography").[36] In 1672, and again in 1681, Newton published a revised, corrected, and amended edition of the _[Geographia Generalis](/wiki/Geographia_Generalis "Geographia Generalis")_ , a geography textbook first published in 1650 by the then-deceased [Bernhardus Varenius](/wiki/Bernhardus_Varenius "Bernhardus Varenius").[37][38] In the _Geographia Generalis,_ Varenius attempted to create a theoretical foundation linking scientific principles to classical concepts in geography, and considered geography to be a mix between science and pure mathematics applied to quantifying features of the Earth.[36][39] While it is unclear if Newton ever lectured in geography, the 1733 Dugdale and Shaw English translation of the book stated Newton published the book to be read by students while he lectured on the subject.[36] The _Geographia Generalis_ is viewed by some as the dividing line between ancient and modern traditions in the [history of geography](/wiki/History_of_geography "History of geography"), and Newton's involvement in the subsequent editions is thought to be a large part of the reason for this enduring legacy.[40]

## Scientific studies

### Mathematics

Newton's work has been said "to distinctly advance every branch of mathematics then studied".[41] His work on [calculus](/wiki/Calculus "Calculus"), usually referred to as fluxions, began in 1664, and by 20 May 1665 as seen in a manuscript, Newton "had already developed the calculus to the point where he could compute the tangent and the curvature at any point of a continuous curve".[42] His work by 1665 amounted to a systematic calculus that unified differentiation and integration, which he applied to the dynamic analysis of algebraic and transcendental curves, an approach described by scholar [Tom Whiteside](/wiki/Tom_Whiteside "Tom Whiteside") as "radically novel, indeed unprecedented" and which later directly informed the theory of central-force orbits in the _[Principia](/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica "Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica")_.[43] Another manuscript of October 1666, is now published among Newton's mathematical papers.[44] Newton recorded a definitive tract of calculus in what is called his "Waste Book".[25] He was self-taught in mathematics and did his research without help, as according to scholar [Richard S. Westfall](/wiki/Richard_S._Westfall "Richard S. Westfall"), "By every indication we have, Newton carried out his education in mathematics and his program of research entirely on his own."[45] His work _[De analysi per aequationes numero terminorum infinitas](/wiki/De_analysi_per_aequationes_numero_terminorum_infinitas "De analysi per aequationes numero terminorum infinitas")_ , sent by [Isaac Barrow](/wiki/Isaac_Barrow "Isaac Barrow") to [John Collins](/wiki/John_Collins_\(mathematician\) "John Collins \(mathematician\)") in June 1669, was identified by Barrow in a letter sent to Collins that August as the work "of an extraordinary genius and proficiency in these things".[46]

Newton later [became involved in a dispute](/wiki/Leibniz%E2%80%93Newton_calculus_controversy "Leibniz–Newton calculus controversy") with the German polymath [Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz](/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz "Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz") over priority in the development of calculus. Both are now credited with independently developing calculus, though with very different [mathematical notations](/wiki/Mathematical_notation "Mathematical notation"). However, it is established that Newton came to develop calculus much earlier than Leibniz.[47][48][49] Despite this, the notation of Leibniz is recognised as the more convenient notation, being adopted by continental European mathematicians, and after 1820, by British mathematicians.[50]

The historian of science [A. Rupert Hall](/wiki/A._Rupert_Hall "A. Rupert Hall") notes that while Leibniz deserves credit for his independent formulation of calculus, Newton was undoubtedly the first to develop it, stating:[51]

> But all these matters are of little weight in comparison with the central truth, which has indeed long been universally recognized, that Newton was master of the essential techniques of the calculus by the end of 1666, almost exactly nine years before Leibniz . . . Newton's claim to have mastered the new infinitesimal calculus long before Leibniz, and even to have written — or at least made a good start upon — a publishable exposition of it as early as 1671, is certainly borne out by copious evidence, and though Leibniz and some of his friends sought to belittle Newton's case, the truth has not been seriously in doubt for the last 250 years.

Hall further notes that in _Principia_ , Newton was able to "formulate and resolve problems by the integration of differential equations" and "in fact, he anticipated in his book many results that later exponents of the calculus regarded as their own novel achievements."[52] Hall notes Newton's rapid development of calculus in comparison to his contemporaries, stating that Newton "well before 1690 . . . had reached roughly the point in the development of the calculus that Leibniz, the two Bernoullis, L'Hospital, Hermann and others had by joint efforts reached in print by the early 1700s".[53]

Despite the convenience of Leibniz's notation, it has been noted that Newton's notation could also have developed multivariate techniques, with his dot notation still widely used in [physics](/wiki/Physics "Physics"). Some academics have noted the richness and depth of Newton's work, such as the physicist [Roger Penrose](/wiki/Roger_Penrose "Roger Penrose"), stating "in most cases Newton's geometrical methods are not only more concise and elegant, they reveal deeper principles than would become evident by the use of those formal methods of calculus that nowadays would seem more direct." The mathematician [Vladimir Arnold](/wiki/Vladimir_Arnold "Vladimir Arnold") stated that "Comparing the texts of Newton with the comments of his successors, it is striking how Newton's original presentation is more modern, more understandable and richer in ideas than the translation due to commentators of his geometrical ideas into the formal language of the calculus of Leibniz."[54]

His work extensively uses calculus in geometric form based on limiting values of the ratios of vanishingly small quantities: in the _Principia_ itself, Newton gave demonstration of this under the name of "the method of first and last ratios"[55] and explained why he put his expositions in this form,[56] remarking also that "hereby the same thing is performed as by the method of indivisibles."[57] Because of this, the _Principia_ has been called "a book dense with the theory and application of the infinitesimal calculus" in modern times[58] and in Newton's time "nearly all of it is of this calculus."[59] His use of methods involving "one or more orders of the infinitesimally small" is present in his _[De motu corporum in gyrum](/wiki/De_motu_corporum_in_gyrum "De motu corporum in gyrum")_ of 1684[60] and in his papers on motion "during the two decades preceding 1684".[61]

It has been argued that Newton had an imprecise or limited understanding of [limits](/wiki/Limit_\(mathematics\) "Limit \(mathematics\)"). However, the mathematician Bruce Pourciau contends that in his _Principia_ , Newton actually demonstrated a more sophisticated understanding of limits than he is generally credited with, including being the first to present an epsilon argument.[62]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Sir_Isaac_Newton_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt.jpg/250px-Sir_Isaac_Newton_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt.jpg)](/wiki/File:Sir_Isaac_Newton_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller,_Bt.jpg)Newton in 1702 by [Godfrey Kneller](/wiki/Godfrey_Kneller "Godfrey Kneller")

Newton had been reluctant to publish his calculus because he feared controversy and criticism.[63] He was close to the Swiss mathematician [Nicolas Fatio de Duillier](/wiki/Nicolas_Fatio_de_Duillier "Nicolas Fatio de Duillier"). In 1691, Duillier started to write a new version of Newton's _Principia_ , and corresponded with Leibniz.[64] In 1693, the relationship between Duillier and Newton deteriorated and the book was never completed.[65] Starting in 1699, Duillier accused Leibniz of plagiarism.[66] The mathematician [John Keill](/wiki/John_Keill "John Keill") accused Leibniz of plagiarism in 1708 in the [Royal Society](/wiki/Royal_Society "Royal Society") journal, thereby deteriorating the situation even more.[67] The dispute then broke out in full force in 1711 when the Royal Society proclaimed in a study that it was Newton who was the true discoverer and labelled Leibniz a fraud; it was later found that Newton wrote the study's concluding remarks on Leibniz. Thus began the bitter controversy which marred the lives of both men until Leibniz's death in 1716.[68]

Newton's first major mathematical discovery was the [generalised binomial theorem](/wiki/Binomial_theorem#Newton's_generalized_binomial_theorem "Binomial theorem"), valid for any exponent, in 1664–65,[69] which has been called "one of the most powerful and significant in the whole of mathematics."[70] He discovered [Newton's identities](/wiki/Newton%27s_identities "Newton's identities") (probably without knowing of earlier work by [Albert Girard](/wiki/Albert_Girard "Albert Girard") in 1629), [Newton's method](/wiki/Newton%27s_method "Newton's method"), the [Newton polygon](/wiki/Newton_polygon "Newton polygon"), and classified [cubic plane curves](/wiki/Cubic_plane_curve "Cubic plane curve") ([polynomials](/wiki/Polynomials "Polynomials") of degree three in two [variables](/wiki/Variable_\(mathematics\) "Variable \(mathematics\)")). Newton is also a founder of the theory of [Cremona transformations](/wiki/Cremona_transformation "Cremona transformation"),[71] and he made substantial contributions to the theory of [finite differences](/wiki/Finite_differences "Finite differences"), with Newton regarded as "the single most significant contributor to finite difference [interpolation](/wiki/Interpolation "Interpolation")", with many formulas created by Newton.[72] He was the first to state [Bézout's theorem](/wiki/B%C3%A9zout%27s_theorem "Bézout's theorem"), and was also the first to use fractional indices and to employ [coordinate geometry](/wiki/Coordinate_geometry "Coordinate geometry") to derive solutions to [Diophantine equations](/wiki/Diophantine_equations "Diophantine equations"). He approximated [partial](/wiki/Series_\(mathematics\) "Series \(mathematics\)") sums of the [harmonic series](/wiki/Harmonic_series_\(mathematics\) "Harmonic series \(mathematics\)") by [logarithms](/wiki/Logarithms "Logarithms") (a precursor to [Euler's summation formula](/wiki/Euler%27s_summation_formula "Euler's summation formula")) and was the first to use [power series](/wiki/Power_series "Power series") with confidence and to revert power series.[73] He introduced the [Puisseux series](/wiki/Puiseux_series "Puiseux series").[74] He also provided the earliest explicit formulation of the general [Taylor series](/wiki/Taylor_series "Taylor series"), which appeared in a 1691-1692 draft of his _De Quadratura Curvarum_.[75][76] He originated the [Newton-Cotes formulas](/wiki/Newton%E2%80%93Cotes_formulas "Newton–Cotes formulas") for [numerical integration](/wiki/Numerical_integration "Numerical integration").[77] Newton's work on infinite series was inspired by [Simon Stevin](/wiki/Simon_Stevin "Simon Stevin")'s decimals.[78] He also initiated the field of [calculus of variations](/wiki/Calculus_of_variations "Calculus of variations"), being the first to formulate and solve a problem in the field, that being [Newton's minimal resistance problem](/wiki/Newton%27s_minimal_resistance_problem "Newton's minimal resistance problem"), which he posed and solved in 1685, later publishing it in _Principia_ in 1687.[79][80] It is regarded as one of the most difficult problems tackled by variational methods prior to the twentieth century.[81] He then used calculus of variations in his solving of the [brachistochrone curve](/wiki/Brachistochrone_curve "Brachistochrone curve") problem in 1697, which was posed by [Johann Bernoulli](/wiki/Johann_Bernoulli "Johann Bernoulli") in 1696, and which he famously solved in a night, thus pioneering the field with his work on the two problems.[82] He was also a pioneer of [vector analysis](/wiki/Vector_calculus "Vector calculus"), as he demonstrated how to apply the [parallelogram law](/wiki/Parallelogram_law "Parallelogram law") for adding various physical quantities and realised that these quantities could be broken down into components in any direction.[83] He is credited with introducing the notion of the [vector](/wiki/Vector_\(mathematics_and_physics\) "Vector \(mathematics and physics\)") in his _Principia_ , by proposing that physical quantities like velocity, acceleration, momentum, and force be treated as directed quantities, thereby making Newton the "true originator of this mathematical object".[84]

Newton was probably first to develop a system of [polar coordinates](/wiki/Polar_coordinate_system "Polar coordinate system") in a strictly analytic sense, with his work in relation to the topic being superior, in both generality and flexibility, to any other during his lifetime. His 1671 _[Method of Fluxions](/wiki/Method_of_Fluxions "Method of Fluxions")_ work preceded the earliest publication on the subject by [Jacob Bernoulli](/wiki/Jacob_Bernoulli "Jacob Bernoulli") in 1691. He is also credited as the originator of [bipolar coordinates](/wiki/Bipolar_coordinates "Bipolar coordinates") in a strict sense.[85][86]

A private manuscript of Newton's which dates to 1664–66 contains what is the earliest known problem in the field of [geometric probability](/wiki/Geometric_probability "Geometric probability"). The problem dealt with the likelihood of a negligible ball landing in one of two unequal sectors of a circle. In analysing this problem, he proposed substituting the enumeration of occurrences with their quantitative assessment, and replacing the estimation of an area's proportion with a tally of points, which has led to him being credited as founding [stereology](/wiki/Stereology "Stereology").[87][88]

Newton was responsible for the modern origin of [Gaussian elimination](/wiki/Gaussian_elimination "Gaussian elimination") in Europe. In 1669 to 1670, Newton wrote that all the algebra books known to him lacked a lesson for solving simultaneous equations, which he then supplied. His notes lay unpublished for decades, but once released, his textbook became the most influential of its kind, establishing the method of substitution and the key terminology of 'extermination' (now known as elimination).[89][90]

In the 1660s and 1670s, Newton found 72 of the 78 "species" of cubic curves and categorised them into four types, systemising his results in later publications. However, a 1690s manuscript later analysed showed that Newton had identified all 78 cubic curves, but chose not to publish the remaining six for unknown reasons.[48][71][77] In 1717, and probably with Newton's help, [James Stirling](/wiki/James_Stirling_\(mathematician\) "James Stirling \(mathematician\)") proved that every cubic was one of these four types. He claimed that the four types could be obtained by [plane projection](/wiki/Projective_plane "Projective plane") from one of them, and this was proved in 1731, four years after his death.[91]

Newton briefly dabbled in [probability](/wiki/Probability "Probability"). In letters with [Samuel Pepys](/wiki/Samuel_Pepys "Samuel Pepys") in 1693, they corresponded over the [Newton–Pepys problem](/wiki/Newton%E2%80%93Pepys_problem "Newton–Pepys problem"), which was a problem about the probability of throwing sixes from a certain number of dice. For it, outcome A was that six dice are tossed with at least one six appearing, outcome B that twelve dice are tossed with at least two sixes appearing, and outcome C in which eighteen dice are tossed with at least three sixes appearing. Newton solved it correctly, choosing outcome A, Pepys incorrectly chose the wrong outcome of C. However, Newton's intuitive explanation for the problem was flawed.[92]

### Optics

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Newton_telescope_replica_1668.jpg/250px-Newton_telescope_replica_1668.jpg)](/wiki/File:Newton_telescope_replica_1668.jpg)A replica of the reflecting telescope Newton presented to the [Royal Society](/wiki/Royal_Society "Royal Society") in 1672 (the first one he made in 1668 was loaned to an instrument maker but there is no further record of what happened to it).[93]

In 1666, Newton observed that the spectrum of colours exiting a [prism](/wiki/Triangular_prism_\(optics\) "Triangular prism \(optics\)") in the position of [minimum deviation](/wiki/Minimum_deviation "Minimum deviation") is oblong, even when the light ray entering the prism is circular, which is to say, the prism refracts different colours by different angles.[94][95] This led him to conclude that colour is a property intrinsic to light – a point which had, until then, been a matter of debate.

From 1670 to 1672, Newton lectured on optics.[96] During this period he investigated the [refraction](/wiki/Refraction "Refraction") of light, demonstrating that the multicoloured image produced by a prism, which he named a [spectrum](/wiki/Spectrum "Spectrum"), could be recomposed into white light by a [lens](/wiki/Lens_\(optics\) "Lens \(optics\)") and a second prism.[97] Modern scholarship has revealed that Newton's analysis and resynthesis of white light owes a debt to [corpuscular](/wiki/Corpuscularianism "Corpuscularianism") alchemy.[98]

In his work on [Newton's rings](/wiki/Newton%27s_rings "Newton's rings") in 1671, he used a method that was unprecedented in the 17th century, as "he _averaged_ all of the differences, and he then calculated the difference between the average and the value for the first ring", in effect introducing a now [standard method](/wiki/Least_squares#Founding "Least squares") for reducing noise in measurements, and which does not appear elsewhere at the time.[99] He extended his "error-slaying method" to studies of equinoxes in 1700, which was described as an "altogether unprecedented method" but differed in that here "Newton required good values for each of the original equinoctial times, and so he devised a method that allowed them to, as it were, self-correct."[100] Newton "invented a certain technique known today as _[linear regression](/wiki/Linear_regression "Linear regression") analysis_",[101] as he wrote the first of the two 'normal equations' known from [ordinary least squares](/wiki/Ordinary_least_squares "Ordinary least squares"), averaged a set of data, 50 years before [Tobias Mayer](/wiki/Tobias_Mayer "Tobias Mayer"), the person originally thought to be the oldest to do so, and he also summed the residuals to zero, forcing the regression line through the average point. He differentiated between two uneven sets of data and may have considered an optimal solution regarding bias, although not in terms of effectiveness.[102]

He showed that coloured light does not change its properties by separating out a coloured beam and shining it on various objects, and that regardless of whether reflected, scattered, or transmitted, the light remains the same colour. Thus, he observed that colour is the result of objects interacting with already-coloured light rather than objects generating the colour themselves. This is known as [Newton's theory of colour](/wiki/Early_life_of_Isaac_Newton#Newton's_theory_of_colour "Early life of Isaac Newton").[103] His 1672 paper on the nature of white light and colours forms the basis for all work that followed on colour and colour vision.[104]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Dispersive_Prism_Illustration.jpg/250px-Dispersive_Prism_Illustration.jpg)](/wiki/File:Dispersive_Prism_Illustration.jpg)Illustration of a [dispersive prism](/wiki/Dispersive_prism "Dispersive prism") separating white light into the colours of the spectrum, as discovered by Newton

From this work, he concluded that the lens of any [refracting telescope](/wiki/Refracting_telescope "Refracting telescope") would suffer from the [dispersion](/wiki/Dispersion_\(optics\) "Dispersion \(optics\)") of light into colours ([chromatic aberration](/wiki/Chromatic_aberration "Chromatic aberration")). As a proof of the concept, he constructed a telescope using reflective mirrors instead of lenses as the [objective](/wiki/Objective_\(optics\) "Objective \(optics\)") to bypass that problem. Building the design, the first known functional reflecting telescope, today known as a [Newtonian telescope](/wiki/Newtonian_telescope "Newtonian telescope"), involved solving the problem of a suitable mirror material and shaping technique.[105] Previous designs for the reflecting telescope were never put into practice or ended in failure, thereby making Newton's telescope the first one truly created.[106] Newton grounded his own mirrors out of a custom composition of highly reflective [speculum metal](/wiki/Speculum_metal "Speculum metal"), using Newton's rings to judge the [quality](/wiki/Quality_\(philosophy\) "Quality \(philosophy\)") of the optics for his telescopes. In late 1668, he was able to produce this first reflecting telescope.[107] It was about eight inches long and it gave a clearer and larger image. Newton reported that he could see the four [Galilean moons](/wiki/Galilean_moons "Galilean moons") of [Jupiter](/wiki/Jupiter "Jupiter") and the [crescent phase](/wiki/Phases_of_Venus "Phases of Venus") of [Venus](/wiki/Venus "Venus") with his new reflecting telescope.[24] In 1671, he was asked for a demonstration of his reflecting telescope by the Royal Society.[108] Their interest encouraged him to publish his notes, _Of Colours_ ,[109] which he later expanded into the work _[Opticks](/wiki/Opticks "Opticks")_. When [Robert Hooke](/wiki/Robert_Hooke "Robert Hooke") criticised some of Newton's ideas, Newton was so offended that he withdrew from public debate. However, the two had brief exchanges in 1679–80, when Hooke, who had been appointed Secretary of the Royal Society,[110] opened a correspondence intended to elicit contributions from Newton to Royal Society transactions,[111] which had the effect of stimulating Newton to work out a proof that the elliptical form of planetary orbits would result from a centripetal force inversely proportional to the square of the radius vector.[112]

In astronomy, Newton is further credited with the realisation that [high-altitude sites](/wiki/List_of_highest_astronomical_observatories#History_of_high_altitude_astronomical_observatories "List of highest astronomical observatories") are superior for observation because they provide the "most serene and quiet Air" above the dense, turbulent atmosphere ("grosser Clouds"), thereby reducing star [twinkling](/wiki/Twinkling "Twinkling").[113][114]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Newton-letter-to-briggs_03.jpg/250px-Newton-letter-to-briggs_03.jpg)](/wiki/File:Newton-letter-to-briggs_03.jpg)Facsimile of a 1682 letter from Newton to [William Briggs](/wiki/William_Briggs_\(physician\) "William Briggs \(physician\)"), commenting on Briggs' _A New Theory of Vision_

Newton argued that light is composed of particles or corpuscles, which were refracted by accelerating into a denser medium. He verged on soundlike waves to explain the repeated pattern of reflection and transmission by thin films (_Opticks_ Bk. II, Props. 12), but still retained his theory of 'fits' that disposed corpuscles to be reflected or transmitted (Props.13). Despite his known preference of a particle theory, Newton noted that light had both particle-like and wave-like properties in _Opticks_ ; he believed that corpuscles must interact with waves in a medium to explain [interference](/wiki/Interference_\(wave_propagation\) "Interference \(wave propagation\)") patterns and the general phenomenon of [diffraction](/wiki/Diffraction "Diffraction").[115][116]

In his _Hypothesis of Light_ of 1675, Newton posited the existence of the [ether](/wiki/Luminiferous_aether "Luminiferous aether") to transmit forces between particles. The contact with the [Cambridge Platonist](/wiki/Cambridge_Platonists "Cambridge Platonists") philosopher [Henry More](/wiki/Henry_More "Henry More") revived his interest in alchemy.[117] He replaced the ether with occult forces based on [Hermetic](/wiki/Hermeticism "Hermeticism") ideas of attraction and repulsion between particles. His contributions to science cannot be isolated from his interest in alchemy.[117] This was at a time when there was no clear distinction between alchemy and science.[118][119]

Newton contributed to the study of [astigmatism](/wiki/Astigmatism_\(optical_systems\) "Astigmatism \(optical systems\)") by helping to erect its mathematical foundation through his discovery that when oblique pencils of light undergo refraction, two distinct image points are created.[120] This would later stimulate the work of [Thomas Young](/wiki/Thomas_Young_\(scientist\) "Thomas Young \(scientist\)").[121]

In 1704, Newton published _Opticks_ , in which he expounded his corpuscular theory of light, and included a set of queries at the end, which were posed as unanswered questions and positive assertions. In line with his corpuscle theory, he thought that normal matter was made of grosser corpuscles and speculated that through a kind of alchemical transmutation, with query 30 stating "Are not gross Bodies and Light convertible into one another, and may not Bodies receive much of their Activity from the Particles of Light which enter their Composition?"[122] Query 6 introduced the concept of a [black body](/wiki/Black_body "Black body").[123][124] _Opticks_ has been referred to as one of the "earliest exemplars of experimental procedure".[45]

In 1699, Newton presented an improved version of his [reflecting quadrant](/wiki/Octant_\(instrument\)#Newton's_reflecting_quadrant "Octant \(instrument\)"), or octant, that he had previously designed to the Royal Society.[125] His design was probably built as early as 1677.[126] It is notable for being the first quadrant to use two mirrors, which greatly improved the accuracy of measurements since it provided a stable view of both the horizon and the celestial body at the same time. His quadrant was built but appears to have not survived to the present. [John Hadley](/wiki/John_Hadley "John Hadley") would later construct his own double-reflecting quadrant that was nearly identical to the one invented by Newton. However, Hadley likely did not know of Newton's original invention, causing confusion regarding originality.[127]

In 1704, Newton constructed and presented a [burning mirror](/wiki/Burning_glass "Burning glass") to the Royal Society. It consisted of seven concave glass mirrors, each about one foot in diameter. It is estimated that it reached a maximum possible radiant energy of 460 W cm⁻², which has been described as "certainly brighter thermally than a thousand Suns (1,000 × 0.065 W cm⁻²)" based on estimating that the intensity of the [Sun](/wiki/Sun "Sun")'s radiation in London in May of 1704 was 0.065 W cm⁻².[128] As a result of the maximum radiant intensity possibly achieved with his mirror he "may have produced the greatest intensity of radiation brought about by human agency before the arrival of [nuclear weapons](/wiki/Nuclear_weapon "Nuclear weapon") in 1945."[129] [David Gregory](/wiki/David_Gregory_\(mathematician\) "David Gregory \(mathematician\)") reported that it caused metals to smoke, boiled [gold](/wiki/Gold "Gold") and brought about the [vitrification](/wiki/Vitrification "Vitrification") of [slate](/wiki/Slate "Slate"). [William Derham](/wiki/William_Derham "William Derham") thought it be to the most powerful burning mirror in Europe at the time.[130]

Newton also made early studies into electricity, as he constructed a primitive form of a frictional [electrostatic generator](/wiki/Electrostatic_generator "Electrostatic generator") using a [glass](/wiki/Glass "Glass") globe,[131] the first to do so with glass instead of [sulfur](/wiki/Sulfur "Sulfur"), which had previously been used by scientists such as [Otto von Guericke](/wiki/Otto_von_Guericke "Otto von Guericke") to construct their globes.[132] He detailed an experiment in 1675 that showed when one side of a glass sheet is rubbed to create an electric charge, it attracts "light bodies" to the opposite side. He interpreted this as evidence that electric forces could pass through glass.[133] Newton also reported to the Royal Society that glass was effective for generating static electricity, classifying it as a "good electric" decades before this property was widely known.[134] His idea in _Opticks_ that optical [reflection](/wiki/Reflection_\(physics\) "Reflection \(physics\)") and [refraction](/wiki/Refraction "Refraction") arise from interactions across the entire surface is seen as a precursor to the field theory of the electric force.[135] He also recognised the crucial role of electricity in nature, believing it to be responsible for various phenomena, including the emission, reflection, refraction, inflection, and heating effects of light. He proposed that electricity was involved in the sensations experienced by the human body, affecting everything from muscle movement to brain function.[136] His theory of nervous transmission had an immense influence on the work of [Luigi Galvani](/wiki/Luigi_Galvani "Luigi Galvani"), as Newton's theory focused on electricity as a possible mediator of nervous transmission, which went against the prevailing Cartesian hydraulic theory of the time. He was also the first to present a clear and balanced theory for how both electrical and chemical mechanisms could work together in the nervous system.[137] Newton's mass-dispersion model, ancestral to the successful use of the [least action principle](/wiki/Action_principles "Action principles"), provided a credible framework for understanding refraction, particularly in its approach to refraction in terms of momentum.[135]

In _Opticks_ , Newton introduced prisms as [beam expanders](/wiki/Beam_expander "Beam expander") and multiple-prism arrays, prismatic configurations that nearly 278 years later were incorporated into [tunable lasers](/wiki/Tunable_laser "Tunable laser"), where [multiple-prism beam expanders](/wiki/Beam_expander#Multiple-prism_beam_expanders "Beam expander") became central to the development of [narrow-linewidth](/wiki/Laser_linewidth "Laser linewidth") systems. The use of these prismatic beam expanders led to the [multiple-prism dispersion theory](/wiki/Multiple-prism_dispersion_theory "Multiple-prism dispersion theory").[10][138]

Newton was the first to theorise the [Goos–Hänchen effect](/wiki/Goos%E2%80%93H%C3%A4nchen_effect "Goos–Hänchen effect"), an [optical phenomenon](/wiki/Optical_phenomenon "Optical phenomenon") in which [linearly polarised](/wiki/Linear_polarization "Linear polarization") light undergoes a small lateral shift when [totally internally reflected](/wiki/Total_internal_reflection "Total internal reflection"). He provided both experimental and theoretical explanations for it using a mechanical model.[139][140][141]

Science came to realise the difference between perception of colour and mathematisable optics. The German poet and scientist [Johann Wolfgang von Goethe](/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe "Johann Wolfgang von Goethe") could not shake the Newtonian foundation but "one hole Goethe did find in Newton's armour, ... Newton had committed himself to the doctrine that refraction without colour was impossible. He, therefore, thought that the object-glasses of telescopes must forever remain imperfect, achromatism and refraction being incompatible. This inference was proved by [Dollond](/wiki/John_Dollond "John Dollond") to be wrong."[142]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton_%284670220%29.jpg/250px-Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton_%284670220%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton_\(4670220\).jpg)Engraving of _Portrait of Newton_ by [John Vanderbank](/wiki/John_Vanderbank "John Vanderbank")

### _Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica_

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/NewtonsPrincipia.jpg/330px-NewtonsPrincipia.jpg)](/wiki/File:NewtonsPrincipia.jpg)Newton's own copy of _[Principia](/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica "Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica")_ with Newton's hand-written corrections for the second edition, now housed in the [Wren Library](/wiki/Wren_Library "Wren Library") at [Trinity College, Cambridge](/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge "Trinity College, Cambridge")

Newton had been developing his theory of gravitation as far back as 1665.[143] In 1679, he returned to his work on [celestial mechanics](/wiki/Celestial_mechanics "Celestial mechanics") by considering gravitation and its effect on the orbits of planets with reference to [Kepler's laws of planetary motion](/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion "Kepler's laws of planetary motion"). Newton's reawakening interest in astronomical matters received further stimulus by the appearance of a comet in the winter of 1680–1681, on which he corresponded with [John Flamsteed](/wiki/John_Flamsteed "John Flamsteed").[144] After his exchanges with [Robert Hooke](/wiki/Robert_Hooke "Robert Hooke"), Newton worked out a proof that the elliptical form of planetary orbits would result from a centripetal force inversely proportional to the square of the radius vector. He shared his results with [Edmond Halley](/wiki/Edmond_Halley "Edmond Halley") and the Royal Society in _[De motu corporum in gyrum](/wiki/De_motu_corporum_in_gyrum "De motu corporum in gyrum")_ , a tract written on about nine sheets which was copied into the Royal Society's Register Book in December 1684.[145] As part of this work, Newton also coined the term [centripetal force](/wiki/Centripetal_force "Centripetal force").[146] This tract contained the nucleus that Newton would develop and expand to form the _Principia_.

The _[Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica](/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica "Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica")_ was published on 5 July 1687 with encouragement and financial help from Halley. In this work, Newton stated the [three universal laws of motion](/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion "Newton's laws of motion"). Together, these laws describe the relationship between any object, the forces acting upon it and the resulting motion, laying the foundation for [classical mechanics](/wiki/Classical_mechanics "Classical mechanics").[7] They contributed to numerous advances during the [Industrial Revolution](/wiki/Industrial_Revolution "Industrial Revolution") and were not improved upon for more than 200 years. Many of these advances still underpin non-relativistic technologies today. Newton used the Latin word _gravitas_ (weight) for the effect that would become known as [gravity](/wiki/Gravity "Gravity"), and formulated the law of [universal gravitation](/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation "Newton's law of universal gravitation").[147] His work achieved the [first great unification in physics](/wiki/Unification_of_theories_in_physics#Unification_of_gravity_on_Earth_with_astronomical_behaviors "Unification of theories in physics").[8] He solved the [two-body problem](/wiki/Two-body_problem "Two-body problem"), and introduced the [three-body problem](/wiki/Three-body_problem "Three-body problem").[148]

In the same work, Newton presented a calculus-like method of geometrical analysis using 'first and last ratios', gave the first analytical determination (based on [Boyle's law](/wiki/Boyle%27s_law "Boyle's law")) of the speed of sound in air, inferred the oblateness of Earth's spheroidal figure, accounted for the precession of the equinoxes as a result of the Moon's gravitational attraction on the Earth's oblateness, initiated the gravitational study of the [irregularities in the motion of the Moon](/wiki/Lunar_theory#Newton "Lunar theory"), provided a theory for the determination of the orbits of comets, and much more.[9][147] Newton's biographer [David Brewster](/wiki/David_Brewster "David Brewster") reported that the complexity of applying his theory of gravity to the motion of the moon was so great it affected Newton's health: "[H]e was deprived of his appetite and sleep" during his work on the problem in 1692–93, and told the astronomer [John Machin](/wiki/John_Machin "John Machin") that "his head never ached but when he was studying the subject". According to Brewster, Halley also told [John Conduitt](/wiki/John_Conduitt "John Conduitt") that when pressed to complete his analysis Newton "always replied that it made his head ache, and _kept him awake so often, that he would think of it no more_ ". [Emphasis in original][149] He provided the first calculation of the [age of Earth](/wiki/Age_of_Earth "Age of Earth") by experiment,[150][151] and also described a precursor to the modern [wind tunnel](/wiki/Wind_tunnel "Wind tunnel").[152]

Newton identified two "principal cases of attraction"—the [inverse-square law](/wiki/Inverse-square_law "Inverse-square law") and a central force proportional to distance—showing that both yield stable conic-section orbits and that spherically symmetric bodies behave as if their mass were concentrated at a point; in modern terms, this linear force law is mathematically equivalent to the force associated with the [cosmological constant](/wiki/Cosmological_constant "Cosmological constant").[153][154]

Through Book II of the _Principia_ , Newton was an important pioneer of [fluid mechanics](/wiki/Fluid_mechanics "Fluid mechanics"), and later analysis has shown that of its 53 propositions almost all are correct, with only two or three open to question.[155] Propositions 1–18 of the book are the first comprehensive treatment of motion under resistance proportional to velocity or its square, leading the scholar [Richard S. Westfall](/wiki/Richard_S._Westfall "Richard S. Westfall") to remark that 'almost without precedent, Newton created the scientific treatment of motion under conditions of resistance, that is, of motion as it is found in the world'.[155] Proposition 15 showed that under an atmosphere whose density falls inversely with distance, a circular-orbiting body subject to drag will trace an equiangular spiral—a result later independently derived by Morduchow and Volpe (1973).[156] In Section IX of Book II, he formulated the [linear relation](/wiki/Newtonian_fluid#Newton's_law_of_viscosity "Newtonian fluid") between viscous resistance and velocity gradient that now defines a [Newtonian fluid](/wiki/Newtonian_fluid "Newtonian fluid"), despite his experiments giving little direct insight into viscosity.[157][158] Newton also discussed the circular motion of fluids and was the first to analyse [Couette flow](/wiki/Couette_flow "Couette flow"), initially in Proposition 51 for a single rotating cylinder and extended in Corollary 2 to the flow between two concentric cylinders.[159][157] Further, he was the first to analyse the resistance of axisymmetric bodies moving through a [rarefied](/wiki/Rarefaction "Rarefaction") medium.[80]

In _Principia_ , Newton provided the first quantitative estimate of the [solar mass](/wiki/Solar_mass "Solar mass"), with later editions incorporating more accurate measurements, bringing his Sun-to-Earth mass ratio calculation close to the modern value.[160][161] He further determined the masses and densities of [Jupiter](/wiki/Jupiter "Jupiter") and [Saturn](/wiki/Saturn "Saturn"), putting all four celestial bodies (Sun, Earth, Jupiter, and Saturn) on the same comparative scale.[162] This achievement by Newton has been called "a supreme expression of the doctrine that one set of physical concepts and principles applies to all bodies on earth, the earth itself, and bodies anywhere throughout the universe".[162]

Newton made clear his [heliocentric](/wiki/Heliocentric "Heliocentric") view of the Solar System—developed in a somewhat modern way because already in the mid-1680s he recognised the "deviation of the Sun" from the centre of gravity of the Solar System.[163] For Newton, it was not precisely the centre of the Sun or any other body that could be considered at rest, but rather "the common centre of gravity of the Earth, the Sun and all the Planets is to be esteem'd the Centre of the World", and this centre of gravity "either is at rest or moves uniformly forward in a right line". (Newton adopted the "at rest" alternative in view of common consent that the centre, wherever it was, was at rest.)[164]

Newton was criticised for introducing "[occult](/wiki/Occult "Occult") agencies" into science because of his postulate of an invisible [force able to act over vast distances](/wiki/Action_at_a_distance_\(physics\) "Action at a distance \(physics\)").[165] Later, in the second edition of the _Principia_ (1713), Newton firmly rejected such criticisms in a concluding [General Scholium](/wiki/General_Scholium "General Scholium"), writing that it was enough that the phenomenon implied a gravitational attraction, as they did; but they did not so far indicate its cause, and it was both unnecessary and improper to frame hypotheses of things that were not implied by the phenomenon. (Here he used what became his famous expression _"[Hypotheses non fingo](/wiki/Hypotheses_non_fingo "Hypotheses non fingo")"_.[166])

With the _Principia_ , Newton became internationally recognised.[167] He acquired a circle of admirers, including the Swiss-born mathematician [Nicolas Fatio de Duillier](/wiki/Nicolas_Fatio_de_Duillier "Nicolas Fatio de Duillier").[168]

### Other significant work

Newton studied heat and energy flow, formulating an [empirical law of cooling](/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_cooling "Newton's law of cooling") which states that the rate at which an object cools is proportional to the temperature difference between the object and its surrounding environment. It was first formulated in 1701, being the first heat transfer formulation and serves as the formal basis of [convective heat transfer](/wiki/Convection_\(heat_transfer\) "Convection \(heat transfer\)"), later being incorporated by [Joseph Fourier](/wiki/Joseph_Fourier "Joseph Fourier") into his work.[11]

Newton was the first to observe and qualitatively describe what would much later be formalised as the [Magnus effect](/wiki/Magnus_effect "Magnus effect"), nearly two centuries before [Heinrich Magnus](/wiki/Heinrich_Gustav_Magnus "Heinrich Gustav Magnus")'s experimental studies. In a 1672 text, Newton recounted watching [tennis](/wiki/Real_tennis "Real tennis") players at Cambridge college and noted how a tennis ball struck obliquely with a spinning motion curved in flight. He explained that the ball's combination of circular and progressive motion caused one side to "press and beat the contiguous air more violently" than the other, thereby producing "a reluctancy and reaction of the air proportionably greater", an astute observation of the pressure differential responsible for lateral deflection.[169][170]

### Philosophy of science

> "Hitherto I have not been able to discover the cause of those properties of gravity from phenomena, and I frame no hypotheses, for whatever is not deduced from the phenomena is to be called an hypothesis; and hypotheses, whether metaphysical or physical, whether of occult qualities or mechanical, have no place in experimental philosophy. In this philosophy particular propositions are inferred from the phenomena, and afterwards rendered general by induction".[57]

— Isaac Newton, _[Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica](/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica "Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica")_

Newton's role as a philosopher was deeply influential, and understanding the philosophical landscape of the late seventeenth and early eighteenth centuries requires recognising his central contributions. Historically, Newton was widely regarded as a core figure in modern philosophy. For example, [Johann Jakob Brucker](/wiki/Johann_Jakob_Brucker "Johann Jakob Brucker")'s _Historia Critica Philosophiae_ (1744), considered the first comprehensive modern history of philosophy, prominently positioned Newton as a central philosophical figure. This portrayal notably shaped the perception of modern philosophy among leading Enlightenment intellectuals, including figures such as [Denis Diderot](/wiki/Denis_Diderot "Denis Diderot"), [Jean le Rond d'Alembert](/wiki/Jean_le_Rond_d%27Alembert "Jean le Rond d'Alembert"), and [Immanuel Kant](/wiki/Immanuel_Kant "Immanuel Kant").[171]

Starting with the second edition of his _Principia_ , Newton included a final section on science philosophy or method. It was here that he wrote his famous line, in Latin, "hypotheses non fingo", which can be translated as "I don't make hypotheses," (the direct translation of "fingo" is "frame", but in context he was advocating against the use of hypotheses in science). Newton's rejection of hypotheses ("hypotheses non fingo") emphasised that he refused to speculate on causes not directly supported by phenomena. Harper explains that Newton's experimental philosophy involves clearly distinguishing hypotheses—unverified conjectures—from propositions established through phenomena and generalised by induction. According to Newton, true scientific inquiry requires grounding explanations strictly on observable data rather than speculative reasoning. Thus, for Newton, proposing hypotheses without empirical backing undermines the integrity of experimental philosophy, as hypotheses should serve merely as tentative suggestions subordinate to observational evidence.[172]

Newton contributed to and refined the [scientific method](/wiki/Scientific_method "Scientific method"). In his work on the properties of light in the 1670s, he showed his rigorous method, which was conducting experiments, taking detailed notes, making measurements, conducting more experiments that grew out of the initial ones, he formulated a theory, created more experiments to test it, and finally described the entire process so other scientists could replicate every step.[173]

In his 1687 _Principia_ , he outlined four rules, which together form the basis of modern science:

  1. "Admit no more causes of natural things than are both true and sufficient to explain their appearances"
  2. "To the same natural effect, assign the same causes"
  3. "Qualities of bodies, which are found to belong to all bodies within experiments, are to be esteemed universal"
  4. "Propositions collected from observation of phenomena should be viewed as accurate or very nearly true until contradicted by other phenomena"[174]



Newton's scientific method went beyond simple prediction in three critical ways, thereby enriching the basic [hypothetico-deductive model](/wiki/Hypothetico-deductive_model "Hypothetico-deductive model"). First, it established a richer ideal of empirical success, requiring phenomena to accurately measure theoretical parameters. Second, it transformed theoretical questions into ones empirically solvable by measurement. Third, it used provisionally accepted propositions to guide research, enabling the method of successive approximations where deviations drive the creation of more accurate models. This robust method of theory-mediated measurements was adopted by his successors for extensions of his theory to [astronomy](/wiki/Astronomy "Astronomy") and remains a foundational element in modern physics.[175]

## Later life

Main article: [Later life of Isaac Newton](/wiki/Later_life_of_Isaac_Newton "Later life of Isaac Newton")

### Royal Mint

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Newton_25.jpg/250px-Newton_25.jpg)](/wiki/File:Newton_25.jpg)Newton in old age in 1712, portrait by [Sir James Thornhill](/wiki/Sir_James_Thornhill "Sir James Thornhill")

In the 1690s, Newton wrote a number of [religious tracts](/wiki/Religious_tracts "Religious tracts") dealing with the literal and symbolic interpretation of the Bible. A manuscript Newton sent to [John Locke](/wiki/John_Locke "John Locke") in which he disputed the fidelity of [1 John 5:7](/wiki/1_John_5:7 "1 John 5:7")—the [Johannine Comma](/wiki/Johannine_Comma "Johannine Comma")—and its fidelity to the original manuscripts of the New Testament, remained unpublished until 1785.[176]

Newton was also a member of the [Parliament of England](/wiki/Parliament_of_England "Parliament of England") for [Cambridge University](/wiki/Cambridge_University_\(UK_Parliament_constituency\) "Cambridge University \(UK Parliament constituency\)") in 1689 and 1701, but according to some accounts his only comments were to complain about a cold draught in the chamber and request that the window be closed.[177] He was, however, noted by the Cambridge diarist [Abraham de la Pryme](/wiki/Abraham_de_la_Pryme "Abraham de la Pryme") to have rebuked students who were frightening locals by claiming that a house was haunted.[178]

Newton moved to London to take up the post of [Warden of the Mint](/wiki/Warden_of_the_Mint "Warden of the Mint") during the reign of [King William III](/wiki/William_III_of_England "William III of England") in 1696, a position that he had obtained through the patronage of [Charles Montagu, 1st Earl of Halifax](/wiki/Charles_Montagu,_1st_Earl_of_Halifax "Charles Montagu, 1st Earl of Halifax"), then [Chancellor of the Exchequer](/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer "Chancellor of the Exchequer"). He took charge of England's great recoining, clashed with [Robert Lucas, 3rd Baron Lucas of Shenfield](/wiki/Robert_Lucas,_3rd_Baron_Lucas_of_Shenfield "Robert Lucas, 3rd Baron Lucas of Shenfield"), the Governor of the Tower,[179] and secured the job of deputy [comptroller](/wiki/Comptroller "Comptroller") of the temporary Chester branch for [Edmond Halley](/wiki/Edmond_Halley "Edmond Halley").[180] Newton became perhaps the best-known [Master of the Mint](/wiki/Master_of_the_Mint "Master of the Mint") upon the death of [Thomas Neale](/wiki/Thomas_Neale "Thomas Neale") in 1699, a position he held for the last 30 years of his life.[181][182] These appointments were intended as [sinecures](/wiki/Sinecure "Sinecure"), but Newton took them seriously. He retired from his Cambridge duties in 1701,[183] and exercised his authority to reform the currency and punish [clippers](/wiki/Methods_of_coin_debasement "Methods of coin debasement") and counterfeiters.

As Warden, and afterwards as Master, of the Royal Mint, Newton estimated that 20 per cent of the coins taken in during the [Great Recoinage of 1696](/wiki/Great_Recoinage_of_1696 "Great Recoinage of 1696") were [counterfeit](/wiki/Counterfeit_money "Counterfeit money"). Counterfeiting was [high treason](/wiki/High_treason_in_the_United_Kingdom "High treason in the United Kingdom"), punishable by the felon being [hanged, drawn and quartered](/wiki/Hanged,_drawn_and_quartered "Hanged, drawn and quartered"). Despite this, convicting even the most flagrant criminals could be extremely difficult, but Newton proved equal to the task.[184]

Disguised as a [habitué](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/habitu%C3%A9 "wikt:habitué") of bars and taverns, he gathered much of that evidence himself.[185] For all the barriers placed to prosecution, and separating the branches of government, [English law](/wiki/English_law "English law") still had ancient and formidable customs of authority. Newton had himself made a [justice of the peace](/wiki/Justice_of_the_peace "Justice of the peace") in all the [home counties](/wiki/Home_counties "Home counties").[186] A draft letter regarding the matter is included in Newton's personal first edition of _Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica_ , which he must have been amending at the time.[187] Then he conducted more than 100 cross-examinations of witnesses, informers, and suspects between June 1698 and Christmas 1699. He successfully prosecuted 28 coiners, including the serial counterfeiter [William Chaloner](/wiki/William_Chaloner "William Chaloner"), who was hanged.[188]

Beyond prosecuting counterfeiters, he improved minting technology and reduced the standard deviation of the weight of guineas from 1.3 grams to 0.75 grams. Starting in 1707, Newton introduced the practice of testing a small sample of coins, a pound in weight, in the [trial of the pyx](/wiki/Trial_of_the_Pyx "Trial of the Pyx"), which helped to reduce the size of admissible error. He ultimately saved the Treasury a then £41,510, roughly £3 million in 2012,[189] with his improvements lasting until the 1770s, thereby increasing the accuracy of British coinage.[190] He greatly increased the productivity of the Mint, as he raised the weekly output of coin from 15,000 pounds to 100,000 pounds.[191] Newton has also been credited with pioneering [time and motion studies](/wiki/Time_and_motion_study "Time and motion study"),[192] although his work was a theoretical calculation of physical capability rather than a standardised industrial productivity model.[193]

Newton's activities at the Mint influenced rising scientific and commercial interests in fields such as [numismatics](/wiki/Numismatics "Numismatics"), [geology](/wiki/Geology "Geology"), [mining](/wiki/Mining "Mining"), [metallurgy](/wiki/Metallurgy "Metallurgy"), and [metrology](/wiki/Metrology "Metrology") in the early 18th century.[193]

Newton held a surprisingly modern view on [economics](/wiki/Economics "Economics"), believing that paper credit, such as government debt, was a practical and wise solution to the limitations of a currency based solely on metal. He argued that increasing the supply of this paper credit could lower interest rates, which would in turn stimulate trade and create employment. Newton also held a radical minority opinion that the value of both metal and paper currency was set by public opinion and trust.[194]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Arms_of_Newton_of_Mickleover%2C_Derbyshire.svg/250px-Arms_of_Newton_of_Mickleover%2C_Derbyshire.svg.png)](/wiki/File:Arms_of_Newton_of_Mickleover,_Derbyshire.svg)[Coat of arms](/wiki/Coat_of_arms "Coat of arms") of the Newton family of [Great Gonerby](/wiki/Great_Gonerby "Great Gonerby"), Lincolnshire, afterwards used by Sir Isaac[195]

Newton was made president of the [Royal Society](/wiki/Royal_Society "Royal Society") in 1703 and an associate of the French [Académie des Sciences](/wiki/French_Academy_of_Sciences "French Academy of Sciences"). In his position at the Royal Society, Newton made an enemy of [John Flamsteed](/wiki/John_Flamsteed "John Flamsteed"), the [Astronomer Royal](/wiki/Astronomer_Royal "Astronomer Royal"), by prematurely publishing Flamsteed's _Historia Coelestis Britannica_ , which Newton had used in his studies.[196]

### Knighthood

In April 1705, Newton was [knighted](/wiki/Knight_Bachelor "Knight Bachelor") by [Queen Anne](/wiki/Anne,_Queen_of_Great_Britain "Anne, Queen of Great Britain") during a royal visit to Trinity College, Cambridge. The knighthood is likely to have been motivated by political considerations connected with the [parliamentary election in May 1705](/wiki/1705_English_general_election "1705 English general election"), rather than any recognition of Newton's scientific work or services as Master of the Mint.[197] Newton was the second scientist to be knighted, after [Francis Bacon](/wiki/Francis_Bacon "Francis Bacon").[198]

As a result of a report written by Newton on 21 September 1717 to the [Lords Commissioners of His Majesty's Treasury](/wiki/Lords_Commissioners_of_the_Treasury "Lords Commissioners of the Treasury"), the bimetallic relationship between gold coins and silver coins was changed by royal proclamation on 22 December 1717, forbidding the exchange of gold guineas for more than 21 silver shillings.[199] This inadvertently resulted in a silver shortage as silver coins were used to pay for imports, while exports were paid for in gold, effectively moving Britain from the [silver standard](/wiki/Silver_standard "Silver standard") to its first [gold standard](/wiki/Gold_standard "Gold standard"). It is a matter of debate as to whether he intended to do this or not.[200] It has been argued that Newton viewed his work at the Mint as a continuation of his alchemical work.[201]

Newton was invested in the [South Sea Company](/wiki/South_Sea_Company "South Sea Company") and lost at least £10,000, and plausibly more than £20,000 (£4.4 million in 2020[202]) when it collapsed in around 1720. Since he was already rich before the bubble, Newton still died rich, at estate value around £30,000.[203]

Toward the end of his life, Newton spent some time at [Cranbury Park](/wiki/Cranbury_Park "Cranbury Park"), near [Winchester](/wiki/Winchester "Winchester"), the country residence of his niece and her husband, though he primarily lived in London.[204][205] His half-niece, [Catherine Barton](/wiki/Catherine_Barton "Catherine Barton"),[206] served as his hostess in social affairs at his house on [Jermyn Street](/wiki/Jermyn_Street "Jermyn Street") in London. In a surviving letter written in 1700 while she was recovering from smallpox, Newton closed with the phrase "your very loving uncle", expressing familial concern in a manner typical of seventeenth-century epistolary style.[207] The historian [Patricia Fara](/wiki/Patricia_Fara "Patricia Fara") notes that the letter's tone is warm and paternal, including medical advice and attention to her appearance during convalescence, rather than conveying any romantic implication.[208]

### Wealth

Newton was an active investor at times, including in the [South Sea Bubble](/wiki/South_Sea_Bubble "South Sea Bubble"). At his death his estate was valued at around £30,000 — nearly £1b in today’s money.[209]

### Death

[![Isaac Newton's death mask](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/PSM_V69_D480_Death_mask_of_isaac_newton.png/250px-PSM_V69_D480_Death_mask_of_isaac_newton.png)](/wiki/File:PSM_V69_D480_Death_mask_of_isaac_newton.png)Death mask of Newton, photographed c. 1906

Newton died in his sleep in London on 20 March 1727 ([NS](/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates "Old Style and New Style dates") 31 March 1727), aged 84.[a] Newton was given a [state funeral](/wiki/State_funeral "State funeral")—the [first](/wiki/State_funerals_in_the_United_Kingdom "State funerals in the United Kingdom") in England for someone recognized primarily for intellectual achievement. The [Lord Chancellor](/wiki/Lord_Chancellor "Lord Chancellor"), two dukes, and three earls bore his pall, with most of the [Royal Society](/wiki/Royal_Society "Royal Society") following. His body lay in state in [Westminster Abbey](/wiki/Westminster_Abbey "Westminster Abbey") for eight days before burial in the nave.[210] Newton was the first scientist to be buried in the abbey.[211] [Voltaire](/wiki/Voltaire "Voltaire") may have been present at his funeral.[212] A bachelor, he had divested much of his estate to relatives during his last years, and died [intestate](/wiki/Intestacy "Intestacy").[213] His papers went to [John Conduitt](/wiki/John_Conduitt "John Conduitt") and [Catherine Barton](/wiki/Catherine_Barton "Catherine Barton").[214]

Shortly after his death, a plaster [death mask](/wiki/Death_mask "Death mask") was moulded of Newton. It was used by the [Flemish](/wiki/Flemings "Flemings") sculptor [John Michael Rysbrack](/wiki/John_Michael_Rysbrack "John Michael Rysbrack") in making a sculpture of Newton.[215] It is now held by the Royal Society.[216]

Newton's hair was posthumously examined and found to contain [mercury](/wiki/Mercury_\(element\) "Mercury \(element\)"), probably resulting from his alchemical pursuits. [Mercury poisoning](/wiki/Mercury_poisoning "Mercury poisoning") could explain Newton's eccentricity in late life.[213]

## Personality

Newton has been described as an incredibly driven and disciplined man who dedicated his life to his work. He is known for having a prodigious appetite for work, which he prioritised above his personal health. Newton also maintained strict control over his physical appetites, being sparing with food and drink and becoming a [vegetarian](/wiki/Vegetarianism "Vegetarianism") later in life.
