While Newton was a secretive and [neurotic](/wiki/Neuroticism "Neuroticism") individual, he is not considered to have been [psychotic](/wiki/Psychosis "Psychosis") or [bipolar](/wiki/Bipolar_disorder "Bipolar disorder"). He has been described as an "incredible polymath" who was "immensely versatile", with some of his first studies relating to a potential [phonetic alphabet](/wiki/Spelling_alphabet "Spelling alphabet") and [universal language](/wiki/Universal_language "Universal language").[217]

Newton's diverse range of interests is seen in his library, which contained 1,752 books that could be identified. A large portion consisted of works on theology (27.2%, or 477 books), followed by alchemy (9.6%, 169 books), mathematics (7.2%, 126 books), physics (3.0%, 52 books), and finally astronomy (1.9%, 33 books). Ultimately, books related to his famous scientific work made up slightly less than 12% of the total collection.[218]

Although it was claimed that he was once engaged,[b] Newton never married. [Voltaire](/wiki/Voltaire "Voltaire"), who was in London at the time of Newton's funeral, said that he "was never sensible to any passion, was not subject to the common frailties of mankind, nor had any commerce with women—a circumstance which was assured me by the physician and surgeon who attended him in his last moments."[220]

Newton had a close friendship with the Swiss mathematician [Nicolas Fatio de Duillier](/wiki/Nicolas_Fatio_de_Duillier "Nicolas Fatio de Duillier"), whom he met in London around 1689;[168] some of their correspondence has survived.[221][222] Their relationship came to an abrupt and unexplained end in 1693, and at the same time Newton suffered a [nervous breakdown](/wiki/Nervous_breakdown "Nervous breakdown"),[223] which included sending wild accusatory letters to his friends [Samuel Pepys](/wiki/Samuel_Pepys "Samuel Pepys") and [John Locke](/wiki/John_Locke "John Locke"). His note to the latter included the charge that Locke had endeavoured to "embroil" him with "woemen & by other means".[224]

Newton appeared to be relatively modest about his achievements, writing in a later memoir, "I do not know what I may appear to the world, but to myself I seem to have been only like a boy playing on the sea-shore, and diverting myself in now and then finding a smoother pebble or a prettier shell than ordinary, whilst the great ocean of truth lay all undiscovered before me."[225] Nonetheless, he could be fiercely competitive and did on occasion hold grudges against his intellectual rivals, not abstaining from personal attacks when it suited him—a common trait found in many of his contemporaries.[217] In a letter to [Robert Hooke](/wiki/Robert_Hooke "Robert Hooke") in February 1675, for instance, he confessed "If I have seen further it is by [standing on the shoulders of giants](/wiki/Standing_on_the_shoulders_of_giants "Standing on the shoulders of giants")."[226] Some historians argued that this, written at a time when Newton and Hooke were disputing over optical discoveries, was an oblique attack on Hooke who was presumably short and hunchbacked, rather than (or in addition to) a statement of modesty.[227] On the other hand, the widely known proverb about standing on the shoulders of giants, found in the 17th-century poet [George Herbert](/wiki/George_Herbert "George Herbert")'s _Jacula Prudentum_ (1651) among others, had as its main point that "a dwarf on a giant's shoulders sees farther of the two", and so in effect place Newton himself rather than Hooke as the 'dwarf' who saw farther.[228]

## Theology

### Religious views

Main articles: [Religious views of Isaac Newton](/wiki/Religious_views_of_Isaac_Newton "Religious views of Isaac Newton") and [Isaac Newton's occult studies](/wiki/Isaac_Newton%27s_occult_studies "Isaac Newton's occult studies")

Although born into an [Anglican](/wiki/Anglicanism "Anglicanism") family, by his thirties Newton had developed unorthodox beliefs,[229] with historian [Stephen Snobelen](/wiki/Stephen_Snobelen "Stephen Snobelen") labelling him a [heretic](/wiki/Heresy "Heresy").[230] Despite this, Newton in his time was considered a knowledgeable and insightful [theologian](/wiki/Theology "Theology") who was respected by his contemporaries, with [Thomas Tenison](/wiki/Thomas_Tenison "Thomas Tenison"), the then [Archbishop of Canterbury](/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury "Archbishop of Canterbury"), telling him "You know more divinity than all of us put together",[231] and the philosopher [John Locke](/wiki/John_Locke "John Locke") describing him as "a very valuable man not onely for his wonderful skill in Mathematicks but in divinity too and his great knowledg in the Scriptures where in I know few his equals".[230] By 1680, his reputation in biblical scholarship was established. [John Mill](/wiki/John_Mill_\(theologian\) "John Mill \(theologian\)") sought his advice on a critical [New Testament](/wiki/New_Testament "New Testament") edition, and the two had a short correspondence on interpreting the early chapters of [Genesis](/wiki/Book_of_Genesis "Book of Genesis") as well. [Thomas Burnet](/wiki/Thomas_Burnet_\(theologian\) "Thomas Burnet \(theologian\)") consulted Newton on drafts of _Telluris theoria sacra_ , and with [Henry More](/wiki/Henry_More "Henry More") he discussed the interpretation of the [Apocalypse](/wiki/Apocalypse "Apocalypse") at Cambridge.[230]

[William Stukeley](/wiki/William_Stukeley "William Stukeley") wrote of Newton’s diligence in reading and studying the Bible:[230]

> No man in England read the Bible more carefully than he did, none study’d it more, as appears by his printed works, by many pieces he left which are not printed, and even by the Bible which he commonly used, thumbd over, as they call it, in an extraordinary degree, with frequency of use.

By 1672, he had started to record his theological researches in notebooks which he showed to no one and which have only been available for public examination since 1972.[232] Over half of what Newton wrote concerned theology and alchemy, and most has never been printed.[232] His writings show extensive knowledge of [early Church](/wiki/Early_Christianity "Early Christianity") texts and reveal that he sided with [Arius](/wiki/Arius "Arius"), who rejected the conventional view of the [Trinity](/wiki/Trinity "Trinity") and was the losing party in the conflict with [Athanasius](/wiki/Athanasius_of_Alexandria "Athanasius of Alexandria") over the [Creed](/wiki/Nicene_Creed "Nicene Creed"). Newton "recognized Christ as a divine mediator between God and man, who was subordinate to the Father who created him."[233] He was especially interested in prophecy, but for him, "the [great apostasy](/wiki/Great_apostasy "Great apostasy") was trinitarianism."[234]

Newton tried unsuccessfully to obtain one of the two fellowships that exempted the holder from the ordination requirement. At the last moment in 1675, he received a government dispensation that excused him and all future holders of the [Lucasian chair](/wiki/Lucasian_Professor_of_Mathematics "Lucasian Professor of Mathematics").[235]

Worshipping [Jesus Christ](/wiki/Jesus "Jesus") as [God](/wiki/God_in_Christianity "God in Christianity") was, in Newton's eyes, [idolatry](/wiki/Idolatry "Idolatry"), an act he believed to be the fundamental [sin](/wiki/Sin "Sin").[236] In 1999, Snobelen wrote, that "Isaac Newton was a heretic. But ... he never made a public declaration of his private faith—which the orthodox would have deemed extremely radical. He hid his faith so well that scholars are still unraveling his personal beliefs." Snobelen concludes that Newton was at least a [Socinian](/wiki/Socinian "Socinian") sympathiser (he owned and had thoroughly read at least eight Socinian books), possibly an [Arian](/wiki/Arianism "Arianism") and almost certainly an [anti-trinitarian](/wiki/Anti-trinitarian "Anti-trinitarian").[230]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Newton-WilliamBlake_crop.jpg/250px-Newton-WilliamBlake_crop.jpg)](/wiki/File:Newton-WilliamBlake_crop.jpg)_[Newton](/wiki/Newton_\(Blake\) "Newton \(Blake\)")_ (1795, detail) by [William Blake](/wiki/William_Blake "William Blake"). Newton is depicted critically as a "divine geometer".[237]

Although the laws of motion and universal gravitation became Newton's best-known discoveries, he warned against using them to view the Universe as a mere machine, as if akin to a great clock. He said, "So then gravity may put the planets into motion, but without the Divine Power it could never put them into such a circulating motion, as they have about the sun".[238]

Along with his scientific fame, Newton's studies of the Bible and of the early [Church Fathers](/wiki/Church_Fathers "Church Fathers") were also noteworthy. Newton wrote works on [textual criticism](/wiki/Textual_criticism "Textual criticism"), most notably _[An Historical Account of Two Notable Corruptions of Scripture](/wiki/An_Historical_Account_of_Two_Notable_Corruptions_of_Scripture "An Historical Account of Two Notable Corruptions of Scripture")_ and _[Observations upon the Prophecies of Daniel, and the Apocalypse of St. John](https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Observations_upon_the_Prophecies_of_Daniel "s:Observations upon the Prophecies of Daniel")_.[239] He placed the crucifixion of Jesus Christ at 3 April, AD 33, which agrees with one traditionally accepted date.[240]

He believed in a rationally [immanent](/wiki/Immanent "Immanent") world, but he rejected the [hylozoism](/wiki/Hylozoism "Hylozoism") implicit in [Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz](/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz "Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz") and [Baruch Spinoza](/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza "Baruch Spinoza"). The ordered and dynamically informed Universe could be understood, and must be understood, by an active reason. In his correspondence, he claimed that in writing the _Principia_ "I had an eye upon such Principles as might work with considering men for the belief of a Deity".[241] He saw evidence of design in the system of the world: "Such a wonderful uniformity in the planetary system must be allowed the effect of choice". But Newton insisted that divine intervention would eventually be required to reform the system, due to the slow growth of instabilities.[242] For this, Leibniz lampooned him: "God Almighty wants to wind up his watch from time to time: otherwise it would cease to move. He had not, it seems, sufficient foresight to make it a perpetual motion."[243]

Newton's position was defended by his follower [Samuel Clarke](/wiki/Samuel_Clarke "Samuel Clarke") in a [famous correspondence](/wiki/Leibniz-Clarke_correspondence "Leibniz-Clarke correspondence"). A century later, [Pierre-Simon Laplace](/wiki/Pierre-Simon_Laplace "Pierre-Simon Laplace")'s work [_Celestial Mechanics_](/wiki/Trait%C3%A9_de_m%C3%A9canique_c%C3%A9leste "Traité de mécanique céleste") had a natural explanation for why the planet orbits do not require periodic divine intervention.[244] The contrast between Laplace's mechanistic worldview and Newton's one is the most strident considering the famous answer which the French scientist gave [Napoleon](/wiki/Napoleon "Napoleon"), who had criticised him for the absence of the Creator in the _Mécanique céleste_ : "Sire, j'ai pu me passer de cette hypothèse" ("Sir, I can do without this hypothesis").[245]

Scholars long debated whether Newton disputed the doctrine of the Trinity. His first biographer, [David Brewster](/wiki/David_Brewster "David Brewster"), who compiled his manuscripts, interpreted Newton as questioning the veracity of some passages used to support the Trinity, but never denying the doctrine of the Trinity as such.[246] In the twentieth century, encrypted manuscripts written by Newton and bought by [John Maynard Keynes](/wiki/John_Maynard_Keynes "John Maynard Keynes") (among others) were deciphered[247] and it became known that Newton did indeed reject Trinitarianism.[230]

Newton broadly endorsed the future [restoration of the Jews to the Land of Israel](/wiki/Christian_Zionism "Christian Zionism") as a component of biblical prophecy while refraining from assigning it a precise date. This view was widely shared among seventeenth- and early eighteenth-century theologians and natural philosophers, including figures connected to the [Royal Society](/wiki/Royal_Society "Royal Society") and the universities. For Newton and his contemporaries, such as Locke and [Daniel Whitby](/wiki/Daniel_Whitby "Daniel Whitby"), belief in a future restoration functioned less as a statement about contemporary Jewish communities than as a theological response to deist critiques, reinforcing the messianic claims of Christianity through appeals to fulfilled and anticipated prophecy.[248]

### Religious thought

Newton and [Robert Boyle](/wiki/Robert_Boyle "Robert Boyle")'s approach to [mechanical philosophy](/wiki/Mechanical_philosophy "Mechanical philosophy") was promoted by [rationalist](/wiki/Rationalist "Rationalist") pamphleteers as a viable alternative to [pantheism](/wiki/Pantheism "Pantheism") and [enthusiasm](/wiki/Enthusiasm "Enthusiasm"). It was accepted hesitantly by orthodox preachers as well as dissident preachers like the [latitudinarians](/wiki/Latitudinarian "Latitudinarian").[249] The clarity and simplicity of science was seen as a way to combat the emotional and [metaphysical](/wiki/Metaphysics "Metaphysics") superlatives of both [superstitious](/wiki/Superstition "Superstition") enthusiasm and the threat of [atheism](/wiki/Atheism "Atheism"),[250] and at the same time, the second wave of English [deists](/wiki/Deism "Deism") used Newton's discoveries to demonstrate the possibility of a "Natural Religion".[251]

The attacks made against pre-[Enlightenment](/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment "Age of Enlightenment") "[magical thinking](/wiki/Magical_thinking "Magical thinking")", and the [mystical elements of Christianity](/wiki/Christian_mysticism "Christian mysticism"), were given their foundation with Boyle's mechanical conception of the universe. Newton gave Boyle's ideas their completion through [mathematical proofs](/wiki/Mathematical_proof "Mathematical proof") and, perhaps more importantly, was very successful in popularising them.[252]

## Alchemy

> Newton was not the first of the age of reason. He was the last of the magicians, the last of the Babylonians and Sumerians, the last great mind which looked out on the visible and intellectual world with the same eyes as those who began to build our intellectual inheritance rather less than 10,000 years ago. Isaac Newton, a posthumous child born with no father on Christmas Day, 1642, was the last wonderchild to whom the Magi could do sincere and appropriate homage.

— [John Maynard Keynes](/wiki/John_Maynard_Keynes "John Maynard Keynes"), "Newton, the Man"[253]

Of an estimated ten million words of writing in Newton's papers, about one million deal with [alchemy](/wiki/Alchemy "Alchemy"). Many of Newton's writings on alchemy are copies of other manuscripts, with his own annotations.[214] Alchemical texts mix artisanal knowledge with philosophical speculation, often hidden behind layers of wordplay, allegory, and imagery to protect craft secrets.[254] Some of the content contained in Newton's papers could have been considered heretical by the church.[214]

In 1888, after spending sixteen years cataloguing Newton's papers, Cambridge University kept a small number and returned the rest to the [Earl of Portsmouth](/wiki/Earl_of_Portsmouth "Earl of Portsmouth"). In 1936, a descendant offered the papers for sale at [Sotheby's](/wiki/Sotheby%27s "Sotheby's").[255] The collection was broken up and sold for a total of about £9,000.[256] [John Maynard Keynes](/wiki/John_Maynard_Keynes "John Maynard Keynes") was one of about three dozen bidders who obtained part of the collection at auction. Keynes went on to reassemble an estimated half of Newton's collection of papers on alchemy before donating his collection to Cambridge University in 1946.[255]

All of Newton's known writings on alchemy are currently being put online in a project undertaken by [Indiana University](/wiki/Indiana_University "Indiana University"): "The Chymistry of Isaac Newton"[257] and has been summarised in a book.[258]

> Newton's fundamental contributions to science include the quantification of gravitational attraction, the discovery that white light is actually a mixture of immutable spectral colors, and the formulation of the calculus. Yet there is another, more mysterious side to Newton that is imperfectly known, a realm of activity that spanned some thirty years of his life, although he kept it largely hidden from his contemporaries and colleagues. We refer to Newton's involvement in the discipline of alchemy, or as it was often called in seventeenth-century England, "chymistry."[257]

In June 2020, two unpublished pages of Newton's notes on [Jan Baptist van Helmont](/wiki/Jan_Baptist_van_Helmont "Jan Baptist van Helmont")'s book on plague, _De Peste_ , were being auctioned online by [Bonhams](/wiki/Bonhams "Bonhams"). Newton's analysis of this book, which he made in Cambridge while protecting himself from [London's 1665–66 epidemic](/wiki/Great_Plague_of_London "Great Plague of London") of the [bubonic plague](/wiki/Bubonic_plague "Bubonic plague"), is the most substantial written statement he is known to have made about the plague, according to Bonhams. As far as the therapy is concerned, Newton writes that "the best is a toad suspended by the legs in a chimney for three days, which at last vomited up earth with various insects in it, on to a dish of yellow wax, and shortly after died. Combining powdered toad with the excretions and serum made into lozenges and worn about the affected area drove away the contagion and drew out the poison".[259]

## Legacy

See also: [Isaac Newton in popular culture](/wiki/Isaac_Newton_in_popular_culture "Isaac Newton in popular culture")

### Recognition

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Tumba_de_Isaac_Newton_-_panoramio_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Tumba_de_Isaac_Newton_-_panoramio_%28cropped%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Tumba_de_Isaac_Newton_-_panoramio_\(cropped\).jpg)Newton's tomb monument in [Westminster Abbey](/wiki/Westminster_Abbey "Westminster Abbey") by [John Michael Rysbrack](/wiki/John_Michael_Rysbrack "John Michael Rysbrack")

The mathematician and physicist [Joseph-Louis Lagrange](/wiki/Joseph_Louis_Lagrange "Joseph Louis Lagrange") frequently asserted that Newton was the greatest [genius](/wiki/Genius "Genius") who ever lived,[260] and once added that Newton was also "the most fortunate, for we cannot find more than once a system of the world to establish."[261] The English poet [Alexander Pope](/wiki/Alexander_Pope "Alexander Pope") wrote the famous [epitaph](/wiki/Epitaph "Epitaph"):

> Nature, and Nature's laws lay hid in night.
>  God said, _Let Newton be!_ and all was light.

But this was not allowed to be inscribed in Newton's monument at Westminster. The epitaph added is as follows:[262]

> _H. S. E. ISAACUS NEWTON Eques Auratus, / Qui, animi vi prope divinâ, / Planetarum Motus, Figuras, / Cometarum semitas, Oceanique Aestus. Suâ Mathesi facem praeferente / Primus demonstravit: / Radiorum Lucis dissimilitudines, / Colorumque inde nascentium proprietates, / Quas nemo antea vel suspicatus erat, pervestigavit. / Naturae, Antiquitatis, S. Scripturae, / Sedulus, sagax, fidus Interpres / Dei O. M. Majestatem Philosophiâ asseruit, / Evangelij Simplicitatem Moribus expressit. / Sibi gratulentur Mortales, / Tale tantumque exstitisse / HUMANI GENERIS DECUS. / NAT. XXV DEC. A.D. MDCXLII. OBIIT. XX. MAR. MDCCXXVI,_

which can be translated as follows:[262]

> Here is buried Isaac Newton, Knight, who by a strength of mind almost divine, and mathematical principles peculiarly his own, explored the course and figures of the planets, the paths of comets, the tides of the sea, the dissimilarities in rays of light, and, what no other scholar has previously imagined, the properties of the colours thus produced. Diligent, sagacious and faithful, in his expositions of nature, antiquity and the holy Scriptures, he vindicated by his philosophy the majesty of God mighty and good, and expressed the simplicity of the Gospel in his manners. Mortals rejoice that there has existed such and so great an ornament of the human race! He was born on 25th December 1642, and died on 20th March 1726.

Science writer John G. Simmons ranked Newton first in _The Scientific 100_ , based on a qualitative assessment in which he ordered the scientists according to overall influence, and described him as "the most influential figure in the history of Western science".[263] Physicist Peter Rowlands described him as "the central figure in the history of science", who "more than anyone else is the source of our great confidence in the power of science."[264] _[New Scientist](/wiki/New_Scientist "New Scientist")_ called Newton "the supreme genius and most enigmatic character in the history of science".[265] The philosopher and historian [David Hume](/wiki/David_Hume "David Hume") also declared that Newton was "the greatest and rarest genius that ever arose for the ornament and instruction of the species".[266] In his home of [Monticello](/wiki/Monticello "Monticello"), [Thomas Jefferson](/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson "Thomas Jefferson"), a [Founding Father](/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States "Founding Fathers of the United States") and [President of the United States](/wiki/President_of_the_United_States "President of the United States"), kept portraits of [John Locke](/wiki/John_Locke "John Locke"), [Sir Francis Bacon](/wiki/Francis_Bacon "Francis Bacon"), and Newton, whom he described as "the three greatest men that have ever lived, without any exception", and who he credited with laying "the foundation of those superstructures which have been raised in the Physical and Moral sciences".[267] The writer and philosopher [Voltaire](/wiki/Voltaire "Voltaire") wrote of Newton that "If all the geniuses of the universe were assembled, Newton should lead the band".[268] The neurologist and psychoanalyst [Ernest Jones](/wiki/Ernest_Jones "Ernest Jones") wrote of Newton as "the greatest genius of all times".[269] The mathematician [Guillaume de l'Hôpital](/wiki/Guillaume_de_l%27H%C3%B4pital "Guillaume de l'Hôpital") had a mythical reverence for Newton, which he expressed with a profound question and statement: "Does Mr. Newton eat, or drink, or sleep like other men? I represent him to myself as a celestial genius, entirely disengaged from matter."[270]

Newton has further been called "the towering figure of the [Scientific Revolution](/wiki/Scientific_Revolution "Scientific Revolution")" and that "In a period rich with outstanding thinkers, Newton was simply the most outstanding." The polymath [Johann Wolfgang von Goethe](/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe "Johann Wolfgang von Goethe") labelled the year in which [Galileo Galilei](/wiki/Galileo_Galilei "Galileo Galilei") died and Newton was born, 1642, as the "[Christmas](/wiki/Christmas "Christmas") of the modern age".[6] In the polymath [Vilfredo Pareto](/wiki/Vilfredo_Pareto "Vilfredo Pareto")'s estimation, Newton was the greatest human being who ever lived.[271] On the bicentennial of Newton's death in 1927, the astronomer [James Jeans](/wiki/James_Jeans "James Jeans") stated that he "was certainly the greatest man of science, and perhaps the greatest intellect, the human race has seen".[268] The physicist Peter Rowlands also notes that Newton was "possibly possessed of the most powerful intellect in the whole of human history".[217] Newton conceived four revolutions—in optics, mathematics, mechanics, and gravity—but also foresaw a fifth in electricity, though he lacked the time and energy in old age to fully accomplish it.[272][273] Newton's work is considered the most influential in bringing forth modern science.[45][274][275][276]

The historian of science [James Gleick](/wiki/James_Gleick "James Gleick") noted that Newton "discovered more of the essential core of human knowledge than anyone before or after", and wrote further:[277]

> He was chief architect of the modern world. He answered the ancient philosophical riddles of light and motion, and he effectively discovered gravity. He showed how to predict the courses of heavenly bodies and so established our place in the cosmos. He made knowledge a thing of substance: quantitative and exact. He established principles, and they are called his laws.

The physicist [Ludwig Boltzmann](/wiki/Ludwig_Boltzmann "Ludwig Boltzmann") called Newton's _Principia_ "the first and greatest work ever written about [theoretical physics](/wiki/Theoretical_physics "Theoretical physics")".[278] Physicist [Stephen Hawking](/wiki/Stephen_Hawking "Stephen Hawking") similarly called _Principia_ "probably the most important single work ever published in the [physical sciences](/wiki/Outline_of_physical_science "Outline of physical science")".[279] The mathematician and physicist Joseph-Louis Lagrange called _Principia_ "the greatest production of the human mind", and noted that "he felt dazed at such an illustration of what man's intellect might be capable".[280]

Physicist [Edward Andrade](/wiki/Edward_Andrade "Edward Andrade") stated that Newton "was capable of greater sustained mental effort than any man, before or since". He also noted the place of Newton in history, stating:[281]

> From time to time in the history of mankind a man arises who is of universal significance, whose work changes the current of human thought or of human experience, so that all that comes after him bears evidence of his spirit. Such a man was [Shakespeare](/wiki/Shakespeare "Shakespeare"), such a man was [Beethoven](/wiki/Beethoven "Beethoven"), such a man was Newton, and, of the three, his kingdom is the most widespread.

The French physicist and mathematician [Jean-Baptiste Biot](/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Biot "Jean-Baptiste Biot") praised Newton's genius, stating that:[282]

> Never was the supremacy of intellect so justly established and so fully confessed . . . In mathematical and in experimental science without an equal and without an example; combining the genius for both in its highest degree.

Despite his rivalry with [Gottfried Wilhem Leibniz](/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz "Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz"), Leibniz still praised the work of Newton, with him responding to a question at a dinner in 1701 from [Sophia Charlotte](/wiki/Sophia_Charlotte_of_Hanover "Sophia Charlotte of Hanover"), the Queen of Prussia, about his view of Newton with:[283][284]

> Taking mathematics from the beginning of the world to the time of when Newton lived, what he had done was much the better half.

The mathematician [E.T. Bell](/wiki/Eric_Temple_Bell "Eric Temple Bell") ranked Newton alongside [Carl Friedrich Gauss](/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss "Carl Friedrich Gauss") and [Archimedes](/wiki/Archimedes "Archimedes") as the three greatest mathematicians of all time,[285] with the mathematician [Donald M. Davis](/wiki/Donald_M._Davis_\(mathematician\) "Donald M. Davis \(mathematician\)") also noting that Newton is generally ranked with the other two as the greatest mathematicians ever.[286] In his 1962 paper from the journal _The Mathematics Teacher_ , the mathematician Walter Crosby Eells sought to objectively create a list that classified the most eminent mathematicians of all time; Newton was ranked first out of a list of the top 100, a position that was statistically confirmed even after taking probable error into account in the study.[287] In his book _Wonders of Numbers_ in 2001, the science editor and author [Clifford A. Pickover](/wiki/Clifford_A._Pickover "Clifford A. Pickover") ranked his top ten most influential mathematicians that ever lived, placing Newton first in the list.[288] In _The Cambridge Companion to Isaac Newton_ (2016), he is described as being "from a very young age, an extraordinary problem-solver, as good, it would appear, as humanity has ever produced".[289] He is ultimately ranked among the top two or three greatest theoretical scientists ever, alongside [James Clerk Maxwell](/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell "James Clerk Maxwell") and [Albert Einstein](/wiki/Albert_Einstein "Albert Einstein"), the greatest mathematician ever alongside Carl F. Gauss, and in the first rank of experimentalists, thereby putting "Newton in a class by himself among empirical scientists, for one has trouble in thinking of any other candidate who was in the first rank of even two of these categories." Also noted is "At least in comparison to subsequent scientists, Newton was also exceptional in his ability to put his scientific effort in much wider perspective".[290] Gauss himself had Archimedes and Newton as his heroes,[291] and used terms such as [_clarissimus_](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/clarissimus "wiktionary:clarissimus") or [_magnus_](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/magnus "wiktionary:magnus") to describe other intellectuals such as great mathematicians and philosophers, but reserved [_summus_](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/summus "wiktionary:summus") for Newton only, and once realising the immense influence of Newton's work on scientists such as Lagrange and [Pierre-Simon Laplace](/wiki/Pierre-Simon_Laplace "Pierre-Simon Laplace"), Gauss then exclaimed that "Newton remains forever the master of all masters!"[280][292]

In his book _Great Physicists_ , the chemist William H. Cropper highlighted the unparalleled genius of Newton, stating:[293]

> On one assessment there should be no doubt: Newton was the greatest creative genius physics has ever seen. None of the other candidates for the superlative (Einstein, Maxwell, Boltzmann, [Gibbs](/wiki/Josiah_Willard_Gibbs "Josiah Willard Gibbs"), and [Feynman](/wiki/Richard_Feynman "Richard Feynman")) has matched Newton's combined achievements as theoretician, experimentalist, _and_ mathematician.

Albert Einstein kept a picture of Newton on his study wall alongside ones of [Michael Faraday](/wiki/Michael_Faraday "Michael Faraday") and of James Clerk Maxwell.[294] Einstein stated that Newton's creation of calculus in relation to his laws of motion was "perhaps the greatest advance in thought that a single individual was ever privileged to make."[295] He also noted the influence of Newton, stating that:[296]

> The whole evolution of our ideas about the processes of nature, with which we have been concerned so far, might be regarded as an organic development of Newton's ideas.

In 1999, an opinion poll of 100 of the day's leading physicists voted Einstein the "greatest physicist ever," with Newton the runner-up, while a parallel survey of rank-and-file physicists ranked Newton as the greatest.[297][298] In 2005, a dual survey of the public and members of Britain's [Royal Society](/wiki/Royal_Society "Royal Society") asked two questions: who made the bigger overall contributions to science and who made the bigger positive contributions to humankind, with the candidates being Newton or Einstein. In both groups, and for both questions, the consensus was that Newton had made the greater overall contributions.[299][300]

In 1999 [_Time_](/wiki/Time_\(magazine\) "Time \(magazine\)") magazine named Newton the [Person of the Century](/wiki/Time_Person_of_the_Year#Special_editions "Time Person of the Year") for the 17th century.[272] Newton placed sixth in the _[100 Greatest Britons](/wiki/100_Greatest_Britons "100 Greatest Britons")_ poll conducted by [BBC](/wiki/BBC "BBC") in 2002. However, in 2003, he was voted as the greatest Briton in a poll conducted by [BBC World](/wiki/BBC_News_\(international_TV_channel\) "BBC News \(international TV channel\)"), with [Winston Churchill](/wiki/Winston_Churchill "Winston Churchill") second.[301] He was voted as the greatest [Cantabrigian](/wiki/Cantabrigian "Cantabrigian") by University of Cambridge students in 2009.[302]

The physicist [Lev Landau](/wiki/Lev_Landau "Lev Landau") [ranked physicists on a logarithmic scale](/wiki/Lev_Landau#Landau's_ranking_of_physicists "Lev Landau") of productivity and genius ranging from 0 to 5. The highest ranking, 0, was assigned to Newton. Einstein was ranked 0.5. A rank of 1 was awarded to the fathers of [quantum mechanics](/wiki/Quantum_mechanics "Quantum mechanics"), such as [Werner Heisenberg](/wiki/Werner_Heisenberg "Werner Heisenberg") and [Paul Dirac](/wiki/Paul_Dirac "Paul Dirac"). Landau, a Nobel prize winner and the discoverer of [superfluidity](/wiki/Superfluidity "Superfluidity"), ranked himself as 2.[303][304]

The [SI derived unit](/wiki/SI_derived_unit "SI derived unit") of [force](/wiki/Force "Force") is named the [newton](/wiki/Newton_\(unit\) "Newton \(unit\)") in his honour.

Most of Newton's surviving scientific and technical papers are kept at [Cambridge University](/wiki/Cambridge_University "Cambridge University"). [Cambridge University Library](/wiki/Cambridge_University_Library "Cambridge University Library") has the largest collection and there are also papers in [Kings College](/wiki/Kings_College,_Cambridge "Kings College, Cambridge"), [Trinity College](/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge "Trinity College, Cambridge"), and the [Fitzwilliam Museum](/wiki/Fitzwilliam_Museum "Fitzwilliam Museum"). There is an archive of theological and alchemical papers in the [National Library of Israel](/wiki/National_Library_of_Israel "National Library of Israel"), and smaller collections at the [Smithsonian Institution](/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution "Smithsonian Institution"), [Stanford University Library](/wiki/Stanford_University_Library "Stanford University Library"), and the [Huntington Library](/wiki/Huntington_Library "Huntington Library"). The [Royal Society](/wiki/Royal_Society "Royal Society") in London also has some manuscripts.[305] The Israel collection was inscribed by [UNESCO](/wiki/UNESCO "UNESCO") on its [Memory of the World International Register](/wiki/Memory_of_the_World_International_Register "Memory of the World International Register") in 2015, recognising the global significance of the documents. The Cambridge and Royal Society collections were added to this inscription in 2017.[306]

### Apple story

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Sapling_of_newton_apple_tree_%28cropped%29.jpg/250px-Sapling_of_newton_apple_tree_%28cropped%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Sapling_of_newton_apple_tree_\(cropped\).jpg)

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Newton%27s_tree%2C_Botanic_Gardens%2C_Cambridge_%28sign%29.jpg/250px-Newton%27s_tree%2C_Botanic_Gardens%2C_Cambridge_%28sign%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Newton%27s_tree,_Botanic_Gardens,_Cambridge_\(sign\).jpg)

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Newtons_apple.jpg/250px-Newtons_apple.jpg)](/wiki/File:Newtons_apple.jpg)

Reputed descendants of Newton's apple tree at (from top to bottom): [Trinity College, Cambridge](/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge "Trinity College, Cambridge"), the [Cambridge University Botanic Garden](/wiki/Cambridge_University_Botanic_Garden "Cambridge University Botanic Garden"), and the [Instituto Balseiro](/wiki/Instituto_Balseiro "Instituto Balseiro") library garden in Argentina

Newton often told the story that he was inspired to formulate his theory of gravitation by watching the fall of an apple from a tree.[307][308] The story is believed to have passed into popular knowledge after being related by [Catherine Barton](/wiki/Catherine_Barton "Catherine Barton"), Newton's niece, to [Voltaire](/wiki/Voltaire "Voltaire").[309] Voltaire then wrote in his _Essay on Epic Poetry_ (1727), "Sir Isaac Newton walking in his gardens, had the first thought of his system of gravitation, upon seeing an apple falling from a tree."[310][311]

Although some question the veracity of the apple story,[312][313] acquaintances of Newton attribute the story to Newton himself, though not the apocryphal version that the apple actually hit Newton's head.[314][315] [William Stukeley](/wiki/William_Stukeley "William Stukeley"), whose manuscript account of 1752 has been made available by the Royal Society, recorded a conversation with Newton in Kensington on 15 April 1726:[316][317]

> we went into the garden, & drank thea under the shade of some appletrees, only he, & myself. amidst other discourse, he told me, he was just in the same situation, as when formerly, the notion of gravitation came into his mind. "why should that apple always descend perpendicularly to the ground," thought he to him self: occasion'd by the fall of an apple, as he sat in a comtemplative mood: "why should it not go sideways, or upwards? but constantly to the earths centre? assuredly, the reason is, that the earth draws it. there must be a drawing power in matter. & the sum of the drawing power in the matter of the earth must be in the earths center, not in any side of the earth. therefore dos this apple fall perpendicularly, or toward the center. if matter thus draws matter; it must be in proportion of its quantity. therefore the apple draws the earth, as well as the earth draws the apple."

[John Conduitt](/wiki/John_Conduitt "John Conduitt"), Newton's assistant at the Royal Mint and husband of Newton's niece, also described the event when he wrote about Newton's life:[318]

> In the year 1666 he retired again from Cambridge to his mother in Lincolnshire. Whilst he was pensively meandering in a garden it came into his thought that the power of gravity (which brought an apple from a tree to the ground) was not limited to a certain distance from earth, but that this power must extend much further than was usually thought. Why not as high as the Moon said he to himself & if so, that must influence her motion & perhaps retain her in her orbit, whereupon he fell a calculating what would be the effect of that supposition.

It is known from his notebooks that Newton was grappling in the late 1660s with the idea that terrestrial gravity extends, in an inverse-square proportion, to the Moon,[319] as [other scientists](/wiki/Newton%E2%80%93Hooke_priority_controversy_for_the_inverse_square_law "Newton–Hooke priority controversy for the inverse square law") had already conjectured. Around 1665, Newton made quantitative analysis, considering the period and distance of the Moon's orbit and considering the timing of objects falling on Earth. Newton did not publish these results at the time because he could not prove that the [Earth's gravity acts as if all its mass were concentrated at its center](/wiki/Shell_theorem "Shell theorem"). That proof took him twenty years.[320]: 13

Detailed analysis of historical accounts backed up by [dendrochronology](/wiki/Dendrochronology "Dendrochronology") and DNA analysis indicate that the sole apple tree in a garden at [Woolsthorpe Manor](/wiki/Woolsthorpe_Manor "Woolsthorpe Manor") was the tree Newton described.[321] The tree blew over in at storm sometime around 1816, regrew from its roots,[322] and continues as a tourist attraction under the care of the [National Trust](/wiki/National_Trust "National Trust").[323][324]

A descendant of the original tree[325] can be seen growing outside the main gate of Trinity College, Cambridge, below the room Newton lived in when he studied there. The [National Fruit Collection](/wiki/National_Fruit_Collection "National Fruit Collection") at [Brogdale](/wiki/Brogdale "Brogdale") in Kent can supply grafts from their tree, which appears identical to [Flower of Kent](/wiki/Flower_of_Kent "Flower of Kent"), a coarse-fleshed cooking variety.[326]

### Commemorations

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Isaac_Newton_statue.jpg/250px-Isaac_Newton_statue.jpg)](/wiki/File:Isaac_Newton_statue.jpg)Newton statue on display at the [Oxford University Museum of Natural History](/wiki/Oxford_University_Museum_of_Natural_History "Oxford University Museum of Natural History") portrays him contemplating the fallen apple.

Newton's monument (1731) can be seen in [Westminster Abbey](/wiki/Westminster_Abbey "Westminster Abbey"), at the north of the entrance to the choir against the choir screen, near his tomb. It was executed by the sculptor [Michael Rysbrack](/wiki/Michael_Rysbrack "Michael Rysbrack") (1694–1770) in white and grey marble with design by the architect [William Kent](/wiki/William_Kent "William Kent").[327] The monument features a figure of Newton reclining on top of a [sarcophagus](/wiki/Sarcophagus "Sarcophagus"), his right elbow resting on several of his great books and his left hand pointing to a scroll with a mathematical design. Above him is a pyramid and a celestial globe showing the signs of the Zodiac and the path of the comet of 1680. A relief panel depicts [putti](/wiki/Putti "Putti") using instruments such as a telescope and prism.[328]

From 1978 until 1988, an image of Newton designed by Harry Ecclestone appeared on Series D £1 banknotes issued by the [Bank of England](/wiki/Bank_of_England "Bank of England") (the last £1 notes to be issued by the Bank of England). Newton was shown on the reverse of the notes holding a book and accompanied by a telescope, a prism and a map of the [Solar System](/wiki/Solar_System "Solar System").[329]

A statue of Isaac Newton, looking at an apple at his feet, can be seen at the [Oxford University Museum of Natural History](/wiki/Oxford_University_Museum_of_Natural_History "Oxford University Museum of Natural History"). A large bronze statue, _[Newton, after William Blake](/wiki/Newton,_after_William_Blake "Newton, after William Blake")_ , by [Eduardo Paolozzi](/wiki/Eduardo_Paolozzi "Eduardo Paolozzi"), dated 1995 and inspired by [William Blake](/wiki/William_Blake "William Blake")'s [etching](/wiki/Newton_\(Blake\) "Newton \(Blake\)"), dominates the piazza of the [British Library](/wiki/British_Library "British Library") in London. A bronze statue of Newton was erected in 1858 in the centre of [Grantham](/wiki/Grantham "Grantham") where he went to school, prominently standing in front of [Grantham Guildhall](/wiki/Grantham_Guildhall "Grantham Guildhall").

The manor house at Woolsthorpe is a Grade I [listed building](/wiki/Listed_building "Listed building") by [Historic England](/wiki/Historic_England "Historic England") through being his birthplace and "where he discovered gravity and developed his theories regarding the refraction of light".[330]

The [Institute of Physics](/wiki/Institute_of_Physics "Institute of Physics"), or IOP, has its highest and most prestigious award, the [Isaac Newton Medal](/wiki/Isaac_Newton_Medal "Isaac Newton Medal"), named after Newton, which is given for world-leading contributions to physics.[331][332] It was first awarded in 2008.

## The Enlightenment

It is held by European philosophers of the [Enlightenment](/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment "Age of Enlightenment") and by historians of the Enlightenment that Newton's publication of the [_Principia_](/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica "Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica") was a turning point in the [Scientific Revolution](/wiki/Scientific_Revolution "Scientific Revolution") and started the Enlightenment. It was Newton's conception of the universe based upon natural and rationally understandable laws that became one of the seeds for Enlightenment ideology.[333] [John Locke](/wiki/John_Locke "John Locke") and [Voltaire](/wiki/Voltaire "Voltaire") applied concepts of natural law to political systems advocating intrinsic rights; the [physiocrats](/wiki/Physiocrat "Physiocrat") and [Adam Smith](/wiki/Adam_Smith "Adam Smith") applied natural conceptions of [psychology](/wiki/Psychology "Psychology") and self-interest to economic systems; and [sociologists](/wiki/Sociology "Sociology") criticised the current [social order](/wiki/Social_order "Social order") for trying to fit history into natural models of [progress](/wiki/Progress_\(history\) "Progress \(history\)").[334] [James Burnett, Lord Monboddo](/wiki/James_Burnett,_Lord_Monboddo "James Burnett, Lord Monboddo") and [Samuel Clarke](/wiki/Samuel_Clarke "Samuel Clarke") resisted elements of Newton's work, but eventually rationalised it to conform with their strong religious views of nature.[335]

## Works

### Published in his lifetime

  * _[De analysi per aequationes numero terminorum infinitas](/wiki/De_analysi_per_aequationes_numero_terminorum_infinitas "De analysi per aequationes numero terminorum infinitas")_ (1669, published 1711)[336]
  * _Of Natures Obvious Laws & Processes in Vegetation_ (unpublished, c. 1671–75)[337]
  * _[De motu corporum in gyrum](/wiki/De_motu_corporum_in_gyrum "De motu corporum in gyrum")_ (1684)[338]
  * _[Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica](/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica "Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica")_ (1687)[339]
  * _[Scala graduum Caloris. Calorum Descriptiones& signa](/wiki/Newton_scale "Newton scale")_ (1701)[340]
  * _[Opticks](/wiki/Opticks "Opticks")_ (1704)[341]
  * _Reports as Master of the Mint_ (1701–1725)[342]
  * _[Arithmetica Universalis](/wiki/Arithmetica_Universalis "Arithmetica Universalis")_ (1707)[342]



### Published posthumously

  * _De mundi systemate_ (_The System of the World_) (1728)[342]
  * _Optical Lectures_ (1728)[342]
  * _[The Chronology of Ancient Kingdoms Amended](/wiki/The_Chronology_of_Ancient_Kingdoms_Amended "The Chronology of Ancient Kingdoms Amended")_ (1728)[342]
  * _Observations on Daniel and The Apocalypse of St. John_ (1733)[342]
  * _[Method of Fluxions](/wiki/Method_of_Fluxions "Method of Fluxions")_ (1671, published 1736)[343]
  * _[An Historical Account of Two Notable Corruptions of Scripture](/wiki/An_Historical_Account_of_Two_Notable_Corruptions_of_Scripture "An Historical Account of Two Notable Corruptions of Scripture")_ (1754)[342]

## References

### Notes

  1. ^ _**a**_ _**b**_ _**c**_ _**d**_ _**e**_ During Newton's lifetime, two calendars were in use in Europe: the [Julian](/wiki/Julian_Calendar "Julian Calendar") ("[Old Style](/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates "Old Style and New Style dates")" calendar in [Protestant](/wiki/Protestant "Protestant") and [Orthodox](/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church "Eastern Orthodox Church") regions, including Britain; and the [Gregorian](/wiki/Gregorian_Calendar "Gregorian Calendar") ("[New Style](/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates "Old Style and New Style dates")") calendar in Roman Catholic Europe. At Newton's birth, Gregorian dates were ten days ahead of Julian dates; thus, his birth is recorded as taking place on 25 December 1642 Old Style, but it can be converted to a New Style (modern) date of 4 January 1643. By the time of his death, the difference between the calendars had increased to eleven days. Moreover the civil or legal year in England began on 25 March, therefore the Newton's death on 20 March was still dated as 1726 O.S. there.
  2. **^** This claim was made by William Stukeley in 1727, in a letter about Newton written to [Richard Mead](/wiki/Richard_Mead "Richard Mead"). [Charles Hutton](/wiki/Charles_Hutton "Charles Hutton"), who in the late eighteenth century collected oral traditions about earlier scientists, declared that there "do not appear to be any sufficient reason for his never marrying, if he had an inclination so to do. It is much more likely that he had a constitutional indifference to the state, and even to the sex in general."[219]
